<$PROJECT DESCRIPTION,OVERVIEW,PROJECTS >
<@3.1@ {PROJECT DESCRIPTION} {OVERVIEW} 
{PROJECTS} 
PROJECTS will be organized into the following categories: 

(1) Primary readings = PRIMARY 
Secondary texts = SECONDARY (3) My own reflections = REFLECTIONS 
(4) Rermarks concerning Aristotle's Greek = LEXICAL 
(5) Pre-modern commentaries, such as Aquinas   or Simplicius = COMMENTARY   
(6) Discussions of Aristotle in philosophical   works that are not primarily about Aristotle   = READINGS   
(7) Flashcards, to help with the assimilation   of key concepts = FLASHCARDS   
// 
All notes for (1) will bn entered in   linear succession, with simple INDEX values   (i.e. 1,2,3,4)   
Notes for (2) will be entered as a series of children. (4.1,4.1.1,4.1.1.1) Other sorts of notes will be subordinated as appropriate

<$CAP TAG,PRIMARY >
<@3.1.1@ {CAP TAG} {PRIMARY} 
The following are the main CAPITALIZED tags that will be used for the {PRIMARY} project.

DEFINITION + [optionally] FIRST, SECOND, THIRD 

EXPLANATION : for a further explanation of a concept, beyond the definition 

ARGUMENT + [optionally] FIRST, SECOND, THIRD : for arguments given by Aristotle 
DOXA: For remarks on the positions of his predecessors 
METHOD: For remarks concerning method 
CRITIQUE: For Aristotle's critique of established positions.

CLARIFICATION = Clarification of the concept 
RECEPTION = Remark about recept history of Aristotle 
BIBLIOGRAPHIC = for giving the orignal bibliographic info...This should always be included for the first note of a text

<$praxis,DEFINITION,agathos,technē,FIRST,book@1,section@1,the Good,PRIMARY@2.0,text@Nicomachean Ethics,bekker@1094.1,methodos,proairesis,chapter@1 >
<@5@ {DEFINITION} {agathos} {the Good} {FIRST} 
Πᾶσα τέχνη καὶ πᾶσα μέθοδος, ὁμοίως δὲ πρᾶξίς τε καὶ (1) προαίρεσις, ἀγαθοῦ τινὸς ἐφίεσθαι δοκεῖ· διὸ καλῶς ἀπεφή- ναντο τἀγαθόν, οὗ πάντ’ ἐφίεται.

Every art {technē} and every investigation {methodos} and likewise every action {praxis} and choice {proairesis} seems to aim at some good. Therefore it is declared well, that the good is that at which all things aim

^:user|S|USER:^

<$phenomenology,telos,doxa,project@Truth Book,REFLECTIONS@1.0,Plato,opinion,REFLECTION >
<@5.1@ Notice how the word "seems" appears here. THIS establishes very clear that Aristotle is talking about the realm of "seeming", {doxa}.

The {telos} is to be found in the first instance in the "realm" of {doxa} {opinion} -- Suggesting {phenomenology}.

This is a very significant point for my own interpretation of how Aristotle will challenge the position of {Plato}, and above all else, the way in which {Plato} conceives of the philosopher as the master of truth, so to speak...

<$lecture@1,Aquinas,REFLECTION,Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics,page@`,wisdom,author@Aquinas,COMMENTARY@1.0,commentaryof@Nicomachean Ethics,title@Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics,translator@Litzinger >
<@5.1.1@ {Aquinas} {Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics} 
{lecture@1} "It is the business of the wise man to order. The reason for this is that wisdom is the most powerful perfection of reason whose characteristic is to know order. Even if the sensitive powers know somethings absolutely, nevertheless to know the order of one thing to another is exclusively the word of intellect or reason" 
 Sicut philosophus dicit in principio metaphysicae, sapientis est ordinare. Cuius ratio est, quia sapientia est potissima perfectio rationis, cuius proprium est cognoscere ordinem. Nam etsi vires sensitivae cognoscant res aliquas absolute, ordinem tamen unius rei ad aliam cognoscere est solius intellectus aut rationis. Invenitur autem duplex ordo in rebus. Unus quidem partium alicuius totius seu alicuius multitudinis adinvicem, sicut partes domus ad invicem ordinantur; alius autem est ordo rerum in finem. Et hic ordo est principalior, quam primus. Nam, ut philosophus dicit in XI metaphysicae, ordo partium exercitus adinvicem, est propter ordinem totius exercitus ad ducem. Ordo autem quadrupliciter ad rationem comparatur. Est enim quidam ordo quem ratio non facit, sed solum considerat, sicut est ordo rerum naturalium. Alius autem est ordo, quem ratio considerando facit in proprio actu, puta cum ordinat conceptus suos adinvicem, et signa conceptuum, quae sunt voces significativae; tertius autem est ordo quem ratio considerando facit in operationibus voluntatis. Quartus autem est ordo quem ratio considerando facit in exterioribus rebus, quarum ipsa est causa, sicut in arca et domo.

^:date|L|['2021-03-05 15:26:35.135150']:^

<$DISTINCTION,author@Aquinas,COMMENTARY@2.0,order,title@Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics >
<@5.1.3@ The four different kinds of {order} {DISTINCTION} 

___1) Order that reason does not establish but only beholds 

___2) Order that reason establishes for its own act of consideration 

___3) Order that reason in deliberating establishes in the operation of its will 

__4) that reason in planning establishes in the external things which it causes, scuh as a chest and a house

<$COMMENTARY@4.0,page@4,compdate@,bekker@1095.2,bekker@1153,author@Aquinas,commentaryof@Nicomachean Ethics,title@Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics,translator@Litzinger >
<@5.1.5@ For truth is the end of speculation 
nam verum est finis speculationis 
Therefore in the speculative intellect he includes teaching by which science is conveyed from teacher to student, while in the practical intellect he locates art which is right reason applies to things to be made, as is stated in the sixth book of this word {bekker@1153} He indicates that the act of the appetitive intellect is choice, and that execution is "actus" He does not mention prudence, which is in the practical reason together with art, because choice is properly directed by prudence. He says therefore that each of these faculties obviously seeks some good as an end

διαφορὰ δέ τις φαίνεται τῶν τελῶν· τὰ μὲν γάρ εἰσιν ἐνέργειαι, τὰ δὲ παρ’ αὐτὰς ἔργα τινά. ὧν δ’ εἰσὶ τέλη τινὰ παρὰ τὰς πράξεις, ἐν τού- (5) τοις βελτίω πέφυκε τῶν ἐνεργειῶν τὰ ἔργα.

<$ARGUMENT,chapter@1,book@1,telos,METAPHOR,text@Nicomachean Ethics,PRIMARY@4.0,archery,EXPLANATION,bekker@1094.2,agathon,section@1 >
<@7@ {EXPLANATION} {ARGUMENT} 
Aristotle's basic argument for why there must  be an ultimate {telos} {agathon} -- "supreme  good"  

_Εἰ δή τι τέλος ἐστὶ τῶν πρακτῶν ὃ δι’ αὑτὸ  βουλόμεθα, τἆλλα δὲ διὰ τοῦτο, καὶ μὴ πάντα  δι’ ἕτερον αἱρούμεθα (πρόεισι γὰρ οὕτω γ’ εἰς  ἄπειρον, πάντα δι’ ἕτερον αἱρούμεθα (πρόεισι  γὰρ οὕτω γ’ εἰς ἄπειρον, (20) ὥστ’ εἶναι κενὴν  καὶ ματαίαν τὴν ὄρεξιν), δῆλον ὡς τοῦτ’ ἂν  εἴη τἀγαθὸν καὶ τὸ ἄριστον. ἆρ’ οὖν καὶ πρὸς  τὸν βίον ἡ γνῶσις αὐτοῦ μεγάλην ἔχει ῥοπήν,  καὶ καθάπερ τοξόται σκοπὸν ἔχοντες μᾶλλον ἂν  τυγχάνοιμεν τοῦ δέοντος; εἰ δ’ οὕτω, πειρατέον  τύπῳ γε περιλαβεῖν αὐτὸ τί ποτ’ ἐστὶ καὶ (25)  τίνος τῶν ἐπιστημῶν ἢ δυνάμεων. δόξειε δ’ ἂν  τῆς κυριω- τάτης καὶ μάλιστα ἀρχιτεκτονικῆς.  τοιαύτη δ’ ἡ πολιτικὴ φαίνεται· τίνας γὰρ εἶναι  χρεὼν τῶν ἐπιστημῶν ἐν ταῖς πό- (1094b) λεσι,  καὶ ποίας ἑκάστους μανθάνειν καὶ μέχρι τίνος,  αὕτη διατάσσει· ὁρῶμεν δὲ καὶ τὰς ἐντιμοτάτας  τῶν δυνάμεων  

[####] 
If therefore among the ends at which our actions  aim there is one which we wish for its own  sake, whgile we wish the others only for the  sake of this, and if we do not choose everything  for the sake of something else (which would  obviously result in a process ad infinitum,  so that all desire would be futile and vain)  it is clear that this one ultimate end must  be the good, and indeed the supreme good. Will  not then a knowledge of this Supreme Good be  also of great practical importance for the  conduct of life? Will it not better enable  us to attain what is fitting, like archers  {archery} {METAPHOR} having a target to aim  at? If this be so, we ought to make an attempt  to determine at all events in outline what  exactly this Supreme Good is, and of which  of the theoretical or practical sciences it  is the object.

<$chapter@2,architektonikēs,book@1,politics,text@Nicomachean Ethics,bekker@1094.2,section@7,PRIMARY@5.0,political science >
<@8@ science of {politics} is the {architektonikēs} 
For this "ordains which of the sceinces are to exist in states, and what branches of knowledge the different classes of citizens are to learn, and up to what point; and we observe that even the most highly esteemed of the faculties, such as strategy, domestic economy, oratory,a re subordinate to the {political science} 

εἰ δ’ οὕτω, πειρατέον τύπῳ γε περιλαβεῖν αὐτὸ τί ποτ’ ἐστὶ καὶ (25) τίνος τῶν ἐπιστημῶν ἢ δυνάμεων. δόξειε δ’ ἂν τῆς κυριω- τάτης καὶ μάλιστα ἀρχιτεκτονικῆς. τοιαύτη δ’ ἡ πολιτικὴ φαίνεται· τίνας γὰρ εἶναι χρεὼν τῶν ἐπιστημῶν ἐν ταῖς πό- (1094b) λεσι, καὶ ποίας ἑκάστους μανθάνειν καὶ μέχρι τίνος, αὕτη διατάσσει· ὁρῶμεν δὲ καὶ τὰς ἐντιμοτάτας τῶν δυνάμεων ὑπὸ ταύτην οὔσας, οἷον στρατηγικὴν οἰκονομικὴν ῥητορικήν· χρωμένης δὲ ταύτης ταῖς λοιπαῖς [πρακτικαῖς] τῶν ἐπιστη- μῶν, ἔτι δὲ νομοθετούσης τί δεῖ πράττειν καὶ τίνων ἀπέχε- (5) σθαι, τὸ ταύτης τέλος περιέχοι ἂν τὰ τῶν ἄλλων, ὥστε τοῦτ’ ἂν εἴη τἀνθρώπινον ἀγαθόν. εἰ γὰρ καὶ ταὐτόν ἐστιν ἑνὶ καὶ πόλει, μεῖζόν γε καὶ τελειότερον τὸ τῆς πόλεως φαίνε- ται καὶ λαβεῖν καὶ σῴζειν· ἀγαπητὸν μὲν γὰρ καὶ ἑνὶ μόνῳ, κάλλιον δὲ καὶ θειότερον ἔθνει καὶ πόλεσιν

<$page@9,COMMENTARY@8.0,author@Aquinas,commentaryof@Nicomachean Ethics,title@Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics,translator@Litzinger >
<@8.1@ But political science dictates to a speculative science only as to activity, not concerning the specification of its proper activity. Political science orders that some teach or learn geometry, and actions of this kind insofar as they are voluntary belong to the matter of ethics and can be ordered to the goal of human living. But the political ruler does not dictate to geometry what conclusions it should draw about a triangle, for this is not subject to the human will nor can it be ordered to human living

<$title@Commens of Aspasius the Philosopher on Aristotle's Ethics,COMMENTARY@12.0,translator@David Konstan,vita activa,vita contemplativa,page@3,author@Aspasius,commentaryof@Nicomachean Ethics,REFLECTION >
<@8.1.1@ The treatment of ethics and especially politics is prior to contemplative philosophy in respect to necessity, but subsequent in respect to value.
Insofar as it is impossible for people to live nobly if they are not temperate, just, and in general well ordered in their character, having settled the emotions of their souls in some sort of equilibrium, the political and ethical treatise would seem necessary and therefore prior (for even if a person should acquire total knowledge and contemplative wisdom, it is of no use if he has not been educated in regard to character). But insofar as wisdom treats the most valuable and divine things, and contemplates the works of nature and, beyond that, other things far better and greater than those constituted by nature, which pertain to first philosophy,1 contemplative philosophy may be said to be prior and more valuable. For as the subjects stand to one another, so too the sciences of these subjects; the things that wisdom deals with are more valuable and greater than those that fall under political and ethical science; thus, wisdom is far more valuable than those sciences.
For in fact, if we were without bodies, there would be no need for our nature to have any other work than contemplation. As it is, however, the nature of the body, which is yoked to bodily pleasures and pains, necessarily causes us to be concerned with temperance, self-control, and many other such virtues which do not plausibly pertain to god because he has no share in bodily pleasures and pains. It is because of the necessity of the body, then, it seems, that we take greatest concern for ethics, since even justice and practical intelligence, in which the divine is believed to share, are far inferior to god. We, however, need them because of the injustices and aggressions that are committed by us against each other, whereas it is plausible that the divine, at all events, exercises only contemplative justice in regard to us and is continually in this state 
// 
{REFLECTION} THE total subordination of the {vita activa} to the {vita contemplativa}

<$COMMENTARY@9.0,author@Aquinas,page@10,commentaryof@Nicomachean Ethics,title@Commentary on Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics,translator@Litzinger >
<@8.2@ he shows that political science is the most important science from the very nature of its special end. It is evident that insofar as a cause is prior and more powerful it extends to more effects. Hence, insofar as the food, which has the nature of a final cause, is more powerful, it extends to more effects. So even though the good be the same objective for one man and for the whole state, it seems much better and more perfect to attain, that is, to procure and persevre the good of the whole state...Nation 
This is said to be more divine because it shows greater likeness to God who is the ultimate cause of all good. BUt this good common to one or to several states is the object of our inquiry, that is, of the particular skill called political science

<$book@1,chapter@4,PRIMARY@8.0,text@Nicomachean Ethics,habit,bekker@1095.2,section@7,METHOD >
<@11@ This is why in order to be a competent student of the Right and the Just, and short of the topics of Politics in general, the pupil is bound to have been trained well in his habits. For the starting-point or first principle is that fact that a thing is so; if this be satisfactorily ascertained, there will be need need also to know the reason why it is so.

{METHOD} {habit} 
τὰ μὲν γὰρ ἡμῖν τὰ δ’ ἁπλῶς. ἴσως οὖν ἡμῖν γε ἀρκτέον ἀπὸ τῶν ἡμῖν γνωρίμων. διὸ δεῖ τοῖς ἔθεσιν ἦχθαι καλῶς τὸν περὶ καλῶν καὶ δικαίων καὶ ὅλως τῶν πολιτικῶν ἀκουσόμε- (5) νον ἱκανῶς. ἀρχὴ γὰρ τὸ ὅτι, καὶ εἰ τοῦτο φαίνοιτο ἀρ- κούντως, οὐδὲν προσδεήσει τοῦ διότι· ὁ δὲ τοιοῦτος ἔχει ἢ λάβοι ἂν ἀρχὰς ῥᾳδίως. ᾧ δὲ μηδέτερον ὑπάρχει τούτων, ἀκουσάτω τῶν Ἡσιόδου·

<$DIVISION,chapter@10,book@1,text@Categories,bekker@11.2,PRIMARY@14.0,opposition >
<@18@ {DIVISION} of different modes of {opposition} 
THIS SECTION SEEMS TO BE REGARDED AS SPURIOUS 
Ὑπὲρ μὲν οὖν τούτων τοσαῦτα λέγεται· εἴρηται δὲ καὶ ὑπὲρ τοῦ κεῖσθαι (10) ἐν τοῖς πρός τι, ὅτι παρωνύμως ἀπὸ τῶν θέσεων λέγεται. ὑπὲρ δὲ τῶν λοιπῶν ν τοῖς πρός τι, ὅτι παρωνύμως ἀπὸ τῶν θέσεων λέγεται. ὑπὲρ δὲ τῶν λοιπῶν, τοῦ τε ποτὲ καὶ τοῦ ποὺ καὶ τοῦ ἔχειν, διὰ τὸ προφανῆ εἶναι οὐδὲν ὑπὲρ αὐτῶν ἄλλο λέγεται ἢ ὅσα ἐν ἀρχῇ ἐῤῥήθη, ὅτι τὸ ἔχειν μὲν σημαίνει τὸ ὑποδεδέσθαι, τὸ ὡπλίσθαι, τὸ δὲ ποὺ οἷον ἐν Λυκείῳ, καὶ τὰ ἄλλα δὲ ὅσα ὑπὲρ αὐτῶν ἐῤῥήθη. -ὑπὲρ μὲν οὖν τῶν προτεθέντων γενῶν ἱκανὰ τὰ εἰρη- (15) μένα· περὶ δὲ τῶν ἀντικειμένων, ποσαχῶς εἴωθε ἀντιτίθεσθαι, ῥητέον 
We have now said enough on the subject of the categories that we proposed, and with opposites next we must deal and the various senses of the word. For we call things opposed in four ways-- (1) first of all, as correlatives are, either term of each pair to the other 

(2) In the next place, as contraries are 

(3) In the third place, as privatives to positives 

(4) lastly, as affirmatives to negatives ''

<$chapter@10,book@1,text@Categories,PRIMARY@15.0,alēthēs/gr,DISTINCTION,pseudos,apophasis/gr,negation,affirmation,kataphasis/gr,true,sumplokēs/gr,opposition,bekker@13.2 >
<@19@ {affirmation} {negation} {opposition} {DISTINCTION} {kataphasis/gr} {apophasis/gr} 
Affirmations and negations are opposed, as is patent, in none of those ways upon which we have already touched. It is here, and here only, indeed, that one opposite must be true {alēthēs/gr} and one must be false {pseudos}. IN the case of the other opposites---contraries, correlatives, positives, and privatives---this will in no wise hold good. Thus of health and disease, which are contraries, neither is true, neither is false. 
To sum up, unless words are combines {sumplokēs/gr} true and false can have no application 
{true} 
Ὅσα δὲ ὡς κατάφασις καὶ ἀπόφασις ἀντίκειται, φανε- (13b) ρὸν ὅτι κατ’ οὐδένα τῶν εἰρημένων τρόπων ἀντίκειται· ἐπὶ μόνων γὰρ τούτων ἀναγκαῖον ἀεὶ τὸ μὲν ἀληθὲς τὸ δὲ ψεῦδος αὐτῶν εἶναι. οὔτε γὰρ ἐπὶ τῶν ἐναντίων ἀναγκαῖον ἀεὶ θάτερον μὲν ἀληθὲς εἶναι θάτερον δὲ ψεῦδος, οὔτε ἐπὶ τῶν πρός τι, οὔτε ἐπὶ τῆς ἕξεως καὶ στερήσεως· οἷον ὑγίεια (5) καὶ νόσος ἐναντία, καὶ οὐδέτερόν γε οὔτε ἀληθὲς οὔτε ψεῦδός ἐστιν· ὡσαύτως δὲ καὶ τὸ διπλάσιον καὶ τὸ ἥμισυ ὡς τὰ πρός τι ἀντίκειται, καὶ οὐκ ἔστιν αὐτῶν οὐδέτερον οὔτε ἀληθὲς οὔτε ψεῦ- δος· οὐδέ γε τὰ κατὰ στέρησιν καὶ ἕξιν, οἷον ἡ ὄψις καὶ ἡ τυφλότης· ὅλως δὲ τῶν κατὰ μηδεμίαν συμπλοκὴν λεγομένων (10) οὐδὲν οὔτε ἀληθὲς οὔτε ψεῦδός ἐστιν· πάντα δὲ τὰ εἰρη οὐδὲν οὔτε ἀληθὲς οὔτε ψεῦδός ἐστιν· πάντα δὲ τὰ εἰρη- μένα ἄνευ συμπλοκῆς λέγεται. οὐ μὴν ἀλλὰ μάλιστα δό- ξειεν ἂν τὸ τοιοῦτο συμβαίνειν ἐπὶ τῶν κατὰ συμπλοκὴν ἐναν- τίων λεγομένων,

<$propositional,Categories,project@Truth Book,REFLECTIONS@3.0,truth,REFLECTION >
<@19.1@ Without doubt a {propositional} concept of {truth} belongs at the core of Aristotle's project. Nevertheless, it seems significant that this is presented already in the {Categories}.

The Categories concerns the language as a means of signification; how meaning is established at the most basic level.

It is equivalent to the language of the cave; in which, as we saw, a "correspondence theory of truth" exists.

But of course, here we also find the kernel of Aristotle's critique of Plato. 
Aristotle's "cave" is not so far from the "truth"... Or indeed: the "thing in itself," being, is articulated through language...

<$apophasis,chapter@10,book@1,text@Categories,PRIMARY@16.0,negation,bekker@13.2,kataphasis,truth,affirmation,REFLECTION >
<@20@ 
To return to {affirmation} and {negation} {kataphasis} {apophasis}. Of these we may say in all cases that one must be false and one true, be the subject existent or not. For if Socrates really exists, 'he is ill' or 'not ill' must be true; 'he is ill' or 'not ill' must be false. And the same, if he does not exist. For, provided he does not exist, it is false to pronounce 'he is ill'; 'he is not ill', however, is true. For, provided he does not exist, it is false to pronounce 'he is ill'; 'he is not ill', however, is true. Thus that one of the trwo must be true and the other be false in all cases will hold of those oppositess only which are in the same sense opposed as affirmative and negative statements.

{truth} // {REFLECTION} 
THIS Seems on the surface like a very curious claim. It doesn't seem to allow the possibility of a vague or ill-formulated sentence...

We might ask, then: ON what basis would it be true? 
It seems to presuppose that a affirmative statement is made true by something "actively" "actually" being the case; Hence, either this exists, or it does not exist. So, if Socrates does not exist, then "Socrates is Ill" is false, since the Socrates-being-Ill is not given. 
The non-existence of the subject of predication entails the non-existence of the truth-making fact

^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-09 09:40:46.377444']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$bekker@14.1,text@Categories,book@1,prōteros,PRIMARY@17.0,priority,number,chapter@12,order >
<@21@ {priority} {prōteros} 
Being prior is said in four different ways: 
(1) In the most proper sense: with reference to time.

(2) Of an order that is fixed and cannot be reversed. Such as "one" is prior to "two" {number} {order} 

{3} with regard to any order whatever. And this is the case in the sciences, and also in the speeches 
{4} Besides the three senses aforesaid, whatsoever is better, more honorable, is said to be naturally prior. 
Thus the common folk, speaking of those whom they hold in esteem or affection, describe them as coming first with them or having prior place in their hearts.

But this use seems the strangest of all. ἔστι μὲν δὴ σχεδὸν ἀλλοτριώτατος τῶν τρόπων οὗτος.

<$PRIMARY@18.0,text@Categories,book@1,DISTINCTION,prior,truth,chapter@12,bekker@14.2 >
<@22@ {prior} {DISTINCTION} But there is also a fifth sense 
Yet another might seem to exist beyhond those we have already mentioned. For where in the case of two things the existence of either implies or necessitates that of the other, that thing which is somehow the cause may, in consequence, fairly be considered as naturally prior to the other. Such cases can clearly be found. The existence of a man, for example, necessitates the truth of the statement wherein we assert his existence. The converse is also the case. For if he exists, then the statement asserting that fact will be true. If the statement, conversely, is true, then the man referred to must exist. The true statement however is nowise the cause of the man's thus existing; and yet his existence would seem in some manner or other the cause of the truth of the true proposition. For the latter is called "true" or "false," as the man thus exists or does not. So it seems that we use the term prior in as many as five different senses.

{truth} // Οἱ μὲν οὖν λεγόμενοι τοῦ προτέρου τρόποι τοσοῦτοί εἰσιν. δόξειε δ’ ἂν καὶ παρὰ τοὺς εἰρημένους ἕτερος (10) εἶναι προτέρου τρόπος· τῶν γὰρ ἀντιστρεφόντων κατὰ τὴν τοῦ εἶναι ἀκολούθησιν τὸ αἴτιον ὁπωσοῦν θατέρῳ τοῦ εἶναι πρότε- ρον εἰκότως φύσει λέγοιτ’ ἄν. ὅτι δ’ ἔστι τινὰ τοιαῦτα, δῆλον· τὸ γὰρ εἶναι ἄνθρωπον ἀντιστρέφει κατὰ τὴν τοῦ εἶ- ναι ἀκολούθησιν πρὸς τὸν ἀληθῆ περὶ αὐτοῦ λόγον· εἰ γὰρ ναι ἀκολούθησιν πρὸς τὸν ἀληθῆ περὶ αὐτοῦ λόγον· εἰ γὰρ (15) ἔστιν ἄνθρωπος, ἀληθὴς ὁ λόγος ᾧ λέγομεν ὅτι ἔστιν ἄν- θρωπος· καὶ ἀντιστρέφει γε, -εἰ γὰρ ἀληθὴς ὁ λόγος ᾧ λέ- γομεν ὅτι ἔστιν ἄνθρωπος, ἔστιν ἄνθρωπος·- ἔστι δὲ ὁ μὲν ἀλη- θὴς λόγος οὐδαμῶς αἴτιος τοῦ εἶναι τὸ πρᾶγμα, τὸ μέντοι πρᾶγμα φαίνεταί πως αἴτιον τοῦ εἶναι ἀληθῆ τὸν λόγον· (20) τῷ γὰρ εἶναι τὸ πρᾶγμα ἢ μὴ ἀληθὴς ὁ λόγος ἢ ψευ- δὴς λέγεται. ὥστε κατὰ πέντε τρόπους πρότερον ἕτερον ἑτέρου λέγοιτ’ ἄν.

<$REFLECTIONS@4.0,project@Truth Book,priority,REFLECTION >
<@22.1@ This discussion of {priority} is remarkable in many ways, but not least of all since it suggests that Aristotle distiguishes between several different modes of speech.
First, you have the "primary" sense of {priority} -- is primacy itself a kind of prioirity -- and then you have two senses derived from this, both of which seem to belong to a more "sceintific" mode of discourse, then you have the "strange" usage of the common people--(what is the sense of the strangeness, one might ask)--and finally you have a very specific philosophical sense...Which is explained with special reference to the question of "truth"...Not that this is the only example...

<$PRIMARY@19.0,book@1,text@Categories,having,diathesis/gr,exis/gr,echein/gr,allotriōtatos/gr,chapter@15,bekker@15.2 >
<@23@ {having} {echein/gr} 
'To have" is said in many ways. We use it of habits {exis/gr} and dispositions {diathesis/gr} and of all the other qualities.

Thus we are said to "have" virtue, to have this or that piece of knowledge.

And then it is use of a quantity, such as the height a man has. 
So it is that we say that a man "has" a stature of three or four cubits. Again, it is used of apparel.

...Moreover, we use it of things that we 'have' on some part of the body, a ring on the finger, for instance.
We employ it of parts of the body...a man 'has' a hand or a foot. It is used in the case of a vessel; a jar will be said to 'have' wine, and a corn-measure to 'have' wheat.

And in cases like these we are thinking of what is contained in the vessel. Once more, we use 'have' of a property, men 'having' houses or fields.

People say that a man 'has' a wife and wife, in like manner, has a husband. This meaning is very far-fetched. {allotriōtatos/gr} When we say that a man has a wife, then we mean that he lives with her merely. 
There may be more senses of 'have'...But the customary meanings, I think, are set forth in the foregoing summary.

// 
Τὸ ἔχειν κατὰ πλείονας τρόπους λέγεται· ἢ γὰρ ὡς ἕξιν καὶ διάθεσιν ἢ ἄλλην τινὰ ποιότητα, -λε- γόμεθα γὰρ ἐπιστήμην ἔχειν καὶ ἀρετήν·- ἢ ὡς ποσόν, οἷον ὃ τυγχάνει τις ἔχων μέγεθος, -λέγεται γὰρ τρίπη- (20) χυ μέγεθος ἔχειν ἢ τετράπηχυ·- ἢ ὡς τὰ περὶ τὸ σῶμα, οἷον ἱμάτιον ἢ χιτῶνα· ἢ ὡς ἐν μορίῳ, οἷον ἐν χειρὶ δα- κτύλιον· ἢ ὡς μέρος, οἷον χεῖρα ἢ πόδα· ἢ ὡς ἐν ἀγγείῳ, οἷον ὁ μέδιμνος τοὺς πυροὺς ἢ τὸ κεράμιον τὸν οἶνον, -οἶνον γὰρ ἔχειν τὸ κεράμιον λέγεται, καὶ ὁ μέδιμνος πυρούς· (25) ταῦτ’ οὖν ἔχειν λέγεται ὡς ἐν ἀγγείῳ·- ἢ ὡς κτῆμα· ἔχειν γὰρ οἰκίαν καὶ ἀγρὸν λεγόμεθα. λεγόμεθα δὲ καὶ γυναῖκα ἔχειν καὶ ἡ γυνὴ ἄνδρα· ἔοικε δὲ ἀλλοτριώτατος ὁ νῦν ῥηθεὶς τρόπος τοῦ ἔχειν εἶναι· οὐδὲν γὰρ ἄλλο τῷ ἔχειν γυ- ναῖκα σημαίνομεν ἢ ὅτι συνοικεῖ. ἴσως δ’ ἂν καὶ ἄλλοι τινὲς (30) φανείησαν τοῦ ἔχειν τρόποι, οἱ δὲ εἰωθότες λέγεσθαι σχεδὸν φανείησαν τοῦ ἔχειν τρόποι, οἱ δὲ εἰωθότες λέγεσθαι σχεδὸν ἅπαντες κατηρίθμηντα

In the usage of Greek so fundamentally different??

<$language,PRIMARY@21.0,DEFINITION,pathāmos,bekker@16.1,book@1,chapter@1,pathemata/gr,graphomena/gr,symbolon/gr,psuchē,phōnē,nature,text@On Interpretation >
<@25@ The {nature} of {language} {DEFINITION} 
Ἔστι μὲν οὖν τὰ ἐν τῇ φωνῇ τῶν ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ παθη- μάτων σύμβολα, καὶ τὰ γραφόμενα τῶν ἐν τῇ φωνῇ.
καὶ ὥσπερ οὐδὲ γράμματα πᾶσι τὰ αὐτά, οὐδὲ φωναὶ αἱ (5) αὐταί· ὧν μέντοι ταῦτα σημεῖα πρώτων, ταὐτὰ πᾶσι πα- θήματα τῆς ψυχῆς, καὶ ὧν ταῦτα ὁμοιώματα πράγματα ἤδη ταὐτά. περὶ μὲν οὖν τούτων εἴρηται ἐν τοῖς περὶ ψυ- χῆς, -ἄλλης γὰρ πραγματείας· 
Words spoken {phōnē} are symbols {symbolon/gr} or signs of affections {pathāmos} in the soul {psuchē}.

WRitten words {graphomena/gr} are signs of words spoken.

Speech and writing not the same for all.

But the {pathemata/gr} of the soul are the same for all. 
But this is discussed in the treatise on the soul.

<$chapter@2,bekker@16.1,DEFINITION,book@1,PRIMARY@24.0,convention,text@On Interpretation >
<@28@ {convention} {DEFINITION} 
ὸ δὲ κατὰ συνθήκην, ὅτι φύσει τῶν ὀνομάτων οὐδέν ἐστιν, ἀλλ’ ὅταν γένηται σύμβολον· ἐπεὶ δηλοῦσί γέ τι καὶ οἱ ἀγράμ- ματοι ψόφοι, οἷον θηρίων, ὧν οὐδέν ἐστιν ὄνομα.

We have already said that a noin signifies this or that by convention. No sound is by nature a noun; it becomes one, becoming a symbol. Inarticulate noices mean something--for example, those made by brute beasts. But no noises of that kind are nouns.

<$bekker@16.2,DEFINITION,organon,book@1,OnInterpretation@1.0,chapter@3,verb,hrēma,PRIMARY@25.0,On Interpretation,text@On Interpretation >
<@29@ {verb} {DEFINITION} 
A verb is a dound which not only conveys a particular meaning, but has a time-reference also.

No part by itself has a meaning.

{hrēma} 
It indicates always something is said or asserted of something. 
Ῥῆμα δέ ἐστι τὸ προσσημαῖνον χρόνον, οὗ μέρος οὐδὲν σημαίνει χωρίς· ἔστι δὲ τῶν καθ’ ἑτέρου λεγομένων σημεῖον.
λέγω δ’ ὅτι προσσημαίνει χρόνον, οἷον ὑγίεια μὲν ὄνομα, τὸ λέγω δ’ ὅτι προσσημαίνει χρόνον, οἷον ὑγίεια μὲν ὄνομα, τὸ δ’ ὑγιαίνει ῥῆμα· προσσημαίνει γὰρ τὸ νῦν ὑπάρχειν. καὶ ἀεὶ τῶν ὑπαρχόντων σημεῖόν ἐστιν, οἷον τῶν καθ’ ὑποκειμένου.

<$bekker@16.2,organon,chapter@3,book@1,OnInterpretation@2.0,PRIMARY@26.0,indefinate verbs,statal verbs,On Interpretation,text@On Interpretation,REFLECTION >
<@30@ {statal verbs} 
Or rather: {indefinate verbs} 
"Is not ill" "is not well" and so on I should not, for my own part, call verbs. Though they certainly have the time-reference and function at all times as predicates, I know of no recognized name. Let us call them (for want of a better) by the name of indefinite verbs, since we use them of all kinds of things non-existence as well as existent.

// {REFLECTION} 
THIS SEEMS VERY STRANGE...Are there not also indefinite verbs with a non-statal meaning. What is the problem with these?? But then-- looking at the earlier paragraph, he seems to privilege the example of statal verbs, which might explain this.

The important point, then, is solely the negation..."I mean of a something predicated of a subject or found present in it"

<@31@ {verbs} by themselves are {nouns}, and they stand for or signify something, for the speaker stops his process of thinking and the mind of the hearer acquisces.

<$bekker@17.1,organon,book@1,PRIMARY@35.0,chapter@6,sophists,OnInterpretation@11.0,kataphasis,sophism,On Interpretation,affirmation,text@On Interpretation >
<@39@ We mean by {affirmation} {kataphasis} a statement affirming one thing of another; we mean by negation a statement denying one thing of another. 

As men can affirm and deny both the presence of that which is present and the presence of that which is absent and this they can do with a reference to times that lie outside the present, whatever a man may affirm, it is possible as well to deny, and whatever a man may deny, it is possible as well to affirm. Thus, it follows, each affirmative statement will have its own opposite negative, just as each negative statement will have its affirmative oppositve. Every such pair of propositions we, therefore, shall call contradictories, always assuming the predicates and subejcts are really the same and the terms used without ambiguity, These and some other provisos are needed in view of the puzzles propounded by importunate sophists. 
κατάφασις δέ ἐστιν ἀπόφανσις τινὸς κατὰ τινός, ἀπό- (25) φασις δέ ἐστιν ἀπόφανσις τινὸς ἀπὸ τινός. ἐπεὶ δὲ ἔστι καὶ τὸ ὑπάρχον ἀποφαίνεσθαι ὡς μὴ ὑπάρχον καὶ τὸ μὴ ὑπάρχον ὡς ὑπάρχον καὶ τὸ ὑπάρχον ὡς ὑπάρχον καὶ τὸ μὴ ὑπάρχον ὡς μὴ ὑπάρχον, καὶ περὶ τοὺς ἐκτὸς δὲ τοῦ νῦν χρόνους ὡσαύτως, ἅπαν ἂν ἐνδέχοιτο καὶ ὃ κατέφησέ (30) τις ἀποφῆσαι καὶ ὃ ἀπέφησε καταφῆσαι· ὥστε δῆλον 
_τις ἀποφῆσαι καὶ ὃ ἀπέφησε καταφῆσαι· ὥστε δῆλον ὅτι πάσῃ καταφάσει ἐστὶν ἀπόφασις ἀντικειμένη καὶ πάσῃ ἀποφάσει κατάφασις. καὶ ἔστω ἀντίφασις τοῦτο, κατάφα- σις καὶ ἀπόφασις αἱ ἀντικείμεναι· λέγω δὲ ἀντικεῖσθαι τὴν τοῦ αὐτοῦ κατὰ τοῦ αὐτοῦ, -μὴ ὁμωνύμως δέ, καὶ ὅσα (35) ἄλλα τῶν τοιούτων προσδιοριζόμεθα πρὸς τὰς σοφιστικὰς ἐνοχλήσεις.

{sophism} {sophists}

<$OnInterpretation@13.0,PRIMARY@37.0,proposition,organon,book@1,dunaton/gr,RELATION,truth,chapter@9,bekker@19.1,archē,On Interpretation,text@On Interpretation,time >
<@41@ In regard to things present or past, propositions, whether positive or negative, are true of necessity or false.

Ἐπὶ μὲν οὖν τῶν ὄντων καὶ γενομένων ἀνάγκη τὴν κατά- φασιν ἢ τὴν ἀπόφασιν ἀληθῆ ἢ ψευδῆ εἶναι· 
REGARDING The {RELATION} of the {truth} of a {proposition} to {time} 
ἐπὶ δὲ τῶν καθ’ ἕκαστα καὶ μελλόντων οὐχ ὁμοίως. εἰ γὰρ πᾶσα κατάφασις ἢ ἀπόφασις ἀληθὴς ἢ ψευδής, καὶ ἅπαν ἀνάγκη ἢ ὑπάρχειν ἢ μὴ ὑπάρχειν· 

When however we come to propositions whose subjects are singular terms, while their predicates refer to the future and not to the present or past, then we find that the case is quite changed. Propositions, whether positive or negative, being themselves true or false, every predicate that we affrim must belong to its subject or not. Hence it is that, if someone declares that a certain event will take place, while another declares that it will note, one will clearly be speaking the truth, while the other clearly will not. Both predicates cannot belong to one subject with regard to the future. For, if it is true to pronounce some particular thing to be white, it must be of necessity white. The reverse of this also holds good. As again it is white or not white, it was true to affirm or deny it. And, if it is not, in fact, white, then to that that it is will be false; it to say that it is will be false, then it follows that the thing is not white. We are driven, therefore, to concluding that all affirmations and denials must either be true or false.

Now, it all this is so, there is nothing that happens by chance or fortuitously; nothing will ever happen. 
ὥστ’ ἀνάγκη τὴν κατάφασιν ἢ τὴν ἀπόφασιν ἀληθῆ εἶναι.
οὐδὲν ἄρα οὔτε ἔστιν οὔτε γίγνεται οὔτε ἀπὸ τύχης οὔθ’ (5) ὁπότερ’ ἔτυχεν, οὐδ’ ἔσται ἢ οὐκ ἔσται, ἀλλ’ ἐξ ἀνάγκης ἅπαν- @1 τα καὶ οὐχ ὁπότερ’ ἔτυχεν (ἢ γὰρ ὁ φὰς ἀληθεύει ἢ ὁ ἀπο- φάς)· 

All this is however impossible. We know from out personal experience that future events may depend on the counsels and actions of men, and that, speaking more broadly, those things that are not uninterrupedly actual exhibit a potentiality, that is, a "may or may not be" 
-ὁρῶμεν γὰρ ὅτι ἔστιν ἀρχὴ τῶν ἐσομένων καὶ ἀπὸ τοῦ βουλεύεσθαι καὶ ἀπὸ τοῦ πρᾶξαί τι, καὶ ὅτι ὅλως ἔστιν ἐν τοῖς μὴ ἀεὶ ἐνεργοῦσι τὸ δυνατὸν εἶναι καὶ μή, ἐν οἷς ἄμφω ἐνδέχεται καὶ τὸ εἶναι καὶ τὸ (10) μὴ εἶναι, ὥστε καὶ τὸ γενέσθαι καὶ τὸ μὴ γενέσθαι· 
{archē} {dunaton/gr} 

Thus it is clear that not everything is or takes place of necessity.

<$organon,book@1,PRIMARY@38.0,chapter@9,bekker@19.1,OnInterpretation@14.0,On Interpretation,text@On Interpretation >
<@42@ What is must needs be when it is; what is not cannot be when it is not. However, not all that exists any more than all that which does not comes about or exists by necessity. That which is must be when it is, does not mean the same thing as to say that all things come about be necessity. And so, too, with that whcih is not. And with two contradictory statements the same thing is found to hold good.

Τὸ μὲν οὖν εἶναι τὸ ὂν ὅταν ᾖ, καὶ τὸ μὴ ὂν μὴ εἶναι @1 ὅταν μὴ ᾖ, ἀνάγκη· οὐ μέντοι οὔτε τὸ ὂν ἅπαν ἀνάγκη εἶναι οὔτε τὸ μὴ ὂν μὴ εἶναι· -οὐ γὰρ ταὐτόν ἐστι τὸ ὂν ἅπαν εἶναι ἐξ (25) ἀνάγκης ὅτε ἔστιν, καὶ τὸ ἁπλῶς εἶναι ἐξ ἀνάγκης· ὁμοίως δὲ καὶ ἐπὶ τοῦ μὴ ὄντος.

That is, all things must be or not be, or must come or not come into being, at this or that time in the future. But we cannot determinately say which alternative must come to pass.

<$compare@Wittgenstein,project@TRUTHBOOK1,being,phenomenological,RELATION,kataphasis,truth,REFLECTIONS@63.0,REFLECTION >
<@42.1@ THIS is an extremely important and subtle point, and gets at the heart of Aristotle's account of {truth} and its {RELATION} to {being}.

Could we say that it involves an distinction between three levels of inquiry: 

(1) Actual states of affairs that can be or not be the case. 
(2) A "metaphysical" claim regarding "determininism" or "non-determinism", or in other words, regarding the way in which states of affairs are connected to one another.

(3) A claim about the "logical structure" of the world. (NOT that Aristotle puts it this way of course. {compare@Wittgenstein}) 

A proposition {kataphasis} is true according to whether it speaks correctly about actual states of affairs. Even when it is speaking of an event that has not yet taken place...It is still either true or false, and not somehoe indeterminate.

And yet, no conclusion can be derived from about the metaphysical structure of the world---whether it is determinate or if there is free will.

The necessity is a "logical" necessity, and this alone... 
BUT ultimately this suggests the {phenomenological} basis of Aristotle's thought..."free will" is phenomenological evident, but so is the "logical structure" of truth...


^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-19 09:32:56.290235']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$DEFINITION,organon,book@1,alētheia,truth,chapter@9,bekker@19.1,PRIMARY@39.0,On Interpretation,text@On Interpretation,OnInterpretation@15.0 >
<@43@ {truth} {alētheia} {DEFINITION} 
ἐπεὶ ὁμοίως οἱ λόγοι ἀληθεῖς ὥσπερ τὰ πράγματα, δῆλον ὅτι ὅσα οὕτως ἔχει ὥστε ὁπότερ’ ἔτυχε καὶ τὰ ἐναντία ἐνδέχεσθαι, ἀνάγκη ὁμοίως ἔχειν καὶ τὴν ἀντίφασιν· 

Thus since sentences are true similarly as the things, it is evident with regard to such things as have change and accept opposites, it is necessary also for the two contradictory statements about them to have the same character.

<@46@ It has been said before that the {soul} {psuch/emacron} has two parts, one rational and the other irrational. Let us now similarly divide the rational part, and let it be assumed that there are two rational faculties, one whereby we contemplate those things whose first principles are invariable, and one whereby we contemplate those things that admit variation: since, on the assumption that knowledge is based on a likeness or affinity of some sort between subject and object, the parts of the soul adapted to the cognition of objects that are of different kinds must themsevles differ in kind. There two rational faculties may be designated as {epistēmonikon/gr} and {logistikon/gr}; since calculation {logizesthai/gr} is the same as deliberation {bouleuetai/gr} and deliberation is never exercised about things that are invariable, so that the Calculative Faculty is a separate part of the rational half of the each

<$chapter@3,project@TRUTHBOOK1,truth,book@6,Nicomachean Ethics,REFLECTIONS@66.0,REFLECTION >
<@47.2@ {REFLECTION} 
The discussion of the intellectual virtues in {book@6} of the {Nicomachean Ethics} seems to be divided into two parts, the first of which concludes with the claim that the attainment of truth is the function of both the epistemic and logistic part of the soul... 
In the second part, which begins {chapter@3}, Aristotle starts the discussion of the virtues afresh, beginning with the assumption that there are "five qualities through which the mind achieves {truth} in affirmation and denial...

i.e. art, scientific knowledge, prudence, wisdom, and intelligence...

<$bekker@1139.1,chapter@2,PRIMARY@45.0,section@3,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,proairēsis,book@6,dianoētikos,NicomacheanEthics@5.0 >
<@49@ The attainment of truth is indeed the function of every part of the intellect, but that of the practical intelligence is the attainment of truth corresponding to right desire 
αὕτη μὲν οὖν ἡ διάνοια καὶ ἡ ἀλήθεια πρακτική· τῆς δὲ θεωρητικῆς διανοίας καὶ μὴ πρακτικῆς μηδὲ ποιητικῆς τὸ εὖ καὶ κακῶς τἀληθές ἐστι καὶ ψεῦδος (τοῦτο γάρ ἐστι παντὸς διανοητικοῦ ἔργον)· τοῦ δὲ πρακτικοῦ καὶ διανοητικοῦ ἀλήθεια ὁμολόγως ἔχουσα τῇ ὀρέξει τῇ (30) ὀρθῇ. πράξεως μὲν οὖν ἀρχὴ προαίρεσις-ὅθεν ἡ κίνησις ὀρθῇ. πράξεως μὲν οὖν ἀρχὴ προαίρεσις-ὅθεν ἡ κίνησις ἀλλ’ οὐχ οὗ ἕνεκα-προαιρέσεως δὲ ὄρεξις καὶ λόγος ὁ ἕνεκά τινος. διὸ οὔτ’ ἄνευ νοῦ καὶ διανοίας οὔτ’ ἄνευ ἠθικῆς ἐστὶν ἕξεως ἡ προαίρεσις· εὐπραξία γὰρ καὶ τὸ ἐναντίον ἐν πράξει ἄνευ διανοίας καὶ ἤθους οὐκ ἔστιν 
Pursuit and avoidance in the sphere of desire correspond to affirmation and denial in the sphere of the intellect. Hence inasmuch as moral virtue is a disposition of the mind in regard to choice, and choice is deliberate desire, {proairēsis} it follows that desire must pursue the same things as principle affirms. We are here speaking of practical thinking, and of the attainment of truth with regard to action; with speculative though, which is not concerned with action or production, right and wrong functioning consist in the attainment of truth and falsehood respectively. 
The attainment of trtuh is indeed the function of every part of the intellect {dianoētikos}, but that of the practical intelligence is the attainment of truth corresponding to right desire

<$NicomacheanEthics@72.0,book@6,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,bekker@1142.2,text@Nicomachean Ethics,doxa,EXPLANATION,chapter@9,section@15,PRIMARY@520.0 >
<@49.12@ {doxa} {EXPLANATION} 
But inasmuch as a bad deliberator makes mistakes and a good deliberator deliberates correctly, it is clear that Deliberative Excellence is some form of correctness; thought it is not correctness of knowledge, nor of opinion. Correctness cannot be predicated of knowledge, any more than error, and correctness of opinion is truth; and also any matter about which one has an opinion has been settled already; [then again Deliberative Excellence necessarily involves conscious calculation. It remains therefore that Deliberative Excellence is correctness in thinking, for thought has not reached the stage of affirmation;] for Opinion has passed beyond the stage of investigation and is a form of affirmation, whereas a man deliberating, whether he deliberates well or badly, is investigating and calculating something.

οὐδὲ δὴ δόξα ἡ εὐβουλία οὐδεμία. ἀλλ’ ἐπεὶ ὁ μὲν κακῶς βουλευό- μενος ἁμαρτάνει, ὁ δ’ εὖ ὀρθῶς βουλεύεται, δῆλον ὅτι ὀρ- θότης τις ἡ εὐβουλία ἐστίν, οὔτ’ ἐπιστήμης δὲ οὔτε δόξης· ἐπιστήμης μὲν γὰρ οὐκ ἔστιν ὀρθότης (οὐδὲ γὰρ ἁμαρτία), (10) δόξης δ’ ὀρθότης ἀλήθεια· ἅμα δὲ καὶ ὥρισται ἤδη πᾶν οὗ δόξα ἐστίν. ἀλλὰ μὴν οὐδ’ ἄνευ λόγου ἡ εὐβουλία. διανοίας ἄρα λείπεται· αὕτη γὰρ οὔπω φάσις· καὶ γὰρ ἡ δόξα οὐ ζήτησις ἀλλὰ φάσις τις ἤδη, ὁ δὲ βουλευόμενος, ἐάν τε εὖ ἐάν τε καὶ κακῶς βουλεύηται, ζητεῖ τι καὶ λογίζεται. (15) ἀλλ’ ὀρθότης τίς ἐστιν ἡ εὐβουλία βουλῆς·

<@49.12.1@ {REFLECTION} 
AN extremely interesting passage ...What is the difference between correctness and error? 
Knowledge can neither be correct nor erroneous (this is as in English)...But it can be true...
Correctness of opinion is truth...SO THEN: truth would not seem to apply as a predicate of knowledge...
[It would make sense, I think, to say that the predication of truth does not apply to knowledge..In so far as true means "correct" rather than "disclosive"...

knowledge is certainly true in the sense of disclosive, but it is not true in the sense of correctness...since correspondence isn't an issue...] 
So opinion is the province of truth...(as belonging to a proposition)

<$PRIMARY@522.0,NicomacheanEthics@74.0,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,bekker@1143.1,chapter@9,book@6,section@1 >
<@49.13@ 
__Again, a man can be said to have deliberated well either generally, or in reference to a particular end. Deliberative Excellence in general is therofer that which leads to correct results with referecne to the end in general, while correctness of deliberation with a view to some particular end is Deliberative excellence of some special kind.
PR 2 PR 3 If therefore to have deliberated well is a characteristic of prudent men, Deliberative Excellence must be correctness of deliberation with regard to what is expedient as a means to the end, a true conception of which constitutes prudence.

ἔτι ἔστι πολὺν χρόνον βουλευόμενον τυχεῖν, τὸν δὲ ταχύ. οὐκοῦν οὐδ’ ἐκείνη πω εὐβουλία, ἀλλ’ ὀρθό- της ἡ κατὰ τὸ ὠφέλιμον, καὶ οὗ δεῖ καὶ ὣς καὶ ὅτε. ἔτι ἔστι καὶ ἁπλῶς εὖ βεβουλεῦσθαι καὶ πρός τι τέλος. ἣ μὲν δὴ ἁπλῶς ἡ πρὸς τὸ τέλος τὸ ἁπλῶς κατορθοῦσα, τὶς δὲ (30) ἡ πρός τι τέλος. εἰ δὴ τῶν φρονίμων τὸ εὖ βεβουλεῦ- σθαι, ἡ εὐβουλία εἴη ἂν ὀρθότης ἡ κατὰ τὸ συμφέρον πρὸς τὸ τέλος, οὗ ἡ φρόνησις ἀληθὴς ὑπόληψίς ἐστιν.

<$chapter@10,DEFINITION,PRIMARY@525.0,phronēsis/gr,prudence,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,understanding,bekker@1143.1,RELATION,sunesis/gr,eusunesia/gr,book@6,NicomacheanEthics@77.0,section@1 >
<@49.14@ {understanding} {DEFINITION} 
{sunesis/gr} {eusunesia/gr} 
Understanding or Good Understanding, the quality in virtue of which we call men 'person of understanding' or 'of good understanding,' is not the same thing as Scientific Knowledge in general (nor yet is it the same as Opinion, for in that case everybody would have understanding), nor is it any one of the particular sciences, as medicine is the science of what pertains to health and geomtery the science concerned with magnitudes. For understanding dow not deal with the things that exist for every and are immutable, nor yet with all of the things that come into existence, buit with those about which one may be in doubt and deliberate. Hence it is concerned with the same objects as {prudence} {phronēsis/gr} {RELATION} 
Ἔστι δὲ καὶ ἡ σύνεσις καὶ ἡ εὐσυνεσία, καθ’ ἃς λέ- (1143a) γομεν συνετοὺς καὶ εὐσυνέτους, οὔθ’ ὅλως τὸ αὐτὸ ἐπιστήμῃ ἢ δόξῃ (πάντες γὰρ ἂν ἦσαν συνετοί) οὔτε τις μία τῶν κατὰ μέρος ἐπιστημῶν, οἷον ἡ ἰατρικὴ περὶ ὑγιεινῶν, ἡ γεω- μετρία περὶ μεγέθη· οὔτε γὰρ περὶ τῶν ἀεὶ ὄντων καὶ ἀκινήτων ἡ σύνεσίς ἐστιν οὔτε περὶ τῶν γιγνομένων ὁτουοῦν, (5) ἀλλὰ περὶ ὧν ἀπορήσειεν ἄν τις καὶ βουλεύσαιτο.

<$kritikē/gr,chapter@10,prudence,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,DIFFERENCE,text@Nicomachean Ethics,understanding,PRIMARY@526.0,NicomacheanEthics@78.0,bekker@1143.1,phronēsis,book@6,sunesis,epitaktikē/gr,section@1 >
<@49.15@ {understanding} {prudence} {DIFFERENCE} {sunesis} {phronēsis} 

Understanding is not however the same thing as prudence; for prudence issues commands, since its end is a statement of what we ought to do or not to do, whereas understanding merely makes judgments. (For understanding is the same as good understanding; a 'man of understanding' means a man of good understanding.') 
διὸ περὶ τὰ αὐτὰ μὲν τῇ φρονήσει ἐστίν, οὐκ ἔστι δὲ τὸ αὐτὸ σύνεσις καὶ φρόνησις. ἡ μὲν γὰρ φρόνησις ἐπιτακτική ἐστιν· τί γὰρ δεῖ πράττειν ἢ μή, τὸ τέλος αὐτῆς ἐστίν· ἡ δὲ σύνεσις κριτικὴ μόνον. ταὐτὸ γὰρ σύνεσις καὶ εὐσυνεσία καὶ συ- (10) νετοὶ καὶ εὐσύνετο 
{epitaktik/emacrongr} = judgment

<$chapter@10,section@20,manthanein/gr,prudence,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,doxa,understanding,NicomacheanEthics@79.0,EXPLANATION,bekker@1143.1,phronēsis,PRIMARY@527.0,xunienai/gr,book@6,epistēmē/gr >
<@49.16@ Thus {understanding} {EXPLANATION} does not mean either the possession or the acquisition of {prudence}; but when we employ the faculty of Opinion {doxa} to judge what another person says about matters that are in the sphere of prudence {phronēsis}, we are said to understand (that is, to judge rightly, for right judgment is the same as good understanding), in the same way as learning {manthanein/gr} a thing is called {xunienai/gr} understanding it when it means exploying the faculty of scientific knowledge {epistēmē/gr}.

In fact, the use of the term understanding to denote theb quality that makes men "persons of good understanding" is derived from understanding as shown in learning; in fact we often use "to learn" in the sense of "to understand"

<$consideration,chapter@10,DEFINITION,NicomacheanEthics@80.0,section@25,suggnōmē/gr,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,gnōmē/gr,bekker@1143.1,alēthous/gr,truth,book@6,PRIMARY@528.0 >
<@49.17@ The quality termed {consideration} {gnōm/emacrongr} is the faculy of judging correctly what is equitable. This is indicated by our saying that the equitable man is specially considerate for others (forgiving), and that it is equitable to show consideration for others (forgiveness) in certain cases; but consideration for others is that consideration which judges rightly what is equitable. Judging rightly meaning judging what is truly equitable. {truth} {alēthous/gr} 
{DEFINITION} of {consideration} ὰρ τὸ μανθάνειν συνιέναι πολλάκις.

ὰρ τὸ μανθάνειν συνιέναι πολλάκις.

__Ἡ δὲ καλουμένη γνώμη, καθ’ ἣν συγγνώμονας καὶ ἔχειν φαμὲν γνώμην, ἡ τοῦ ἐπιεικοῦς ἐστὶ κρίσις ὀρθή. (20) σημεῖον δέ· τὸν γὰρ ἐπιεικῆ μάλιστά φαμεν εἶναι συγ- γνωμονικόν, καὶ ἐπιεικὲς τὸ ἔχειν περὶ ἔνια συγγνώμην.
ἡ δὲ συγγνώμη γνώμη ἐστὶ κριτικὴ τοῦ ἐπιεικοῦς ὀρθή· ὀρθὴ δ’ ἡ τοῦ ἀληθοῦς 

γνώμ-η, ἡ, means of knowing: hence, mark, token, Thgn.60 (pl.); of the teeth (cf. γνώμων III), Arist.HA576b15.
II. organ by which one perceives or knows, intelligence, 1. thought, judgement (τῆς ψυχῆς ἡ γ. Pl.Lg.672b), ἐκμαθεῖν ψυχήν τε καὶ φρόνημα καὶ γ. S. Ant.176: acc. abs., γνώμην ἱκανός intelligent, Hdt.3.4; γ. ἀγαθός, κακός, S.OT687, Ph.910; τοιάδε τὴν γ. Id.El.1021; κατὰ γ. ἴδρις Id.OT1087 (lyr.); γνώμᾳ διπλόαν θέτο βουλάν Pi.N.10.89; γνώμῃ μαθεῖν τι S.OC403; γνώμῃ κυρήσας Id.OT398; γνώμῃ φρενῶν, opp. ὀργῇ, ib.524; γνώμης ξύνεσις Th.1.75; γνώμης μᾶλλον ἐφόδῳ ἢ ἰσχύος Id.3.11; ταῖς γ. καὶ τοῖς σώμασι σφάλλεσθαι X. <$nous,NicomacheanEthics@83.0,apodeixis,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,section@10,text@Nicomachean Ethics,praktikais,protos horos/syntagm,gnōm/emacron,horos,book@6,logos,bekker@1143.2,chapter@11,PRIMARY@531.0 >
<@49.18@ All these qualities, it is reasonable to say, refer to the same thing; indeed we attribute Considerateness, Understanding, Prudence, and Intelligence to the same person when we say of people that they 'are old enough to show consideration and intelligence,' and are prudent and understanding persons. FOr all these faculties deal with ultimate and particular things; and a man has understanding and is considerate, or considerate for others, when he is a good judge of the matters in regard to which prudence is displayed; because equitable actions are common to all good men in their behavior toward others, while on the other hand all matters of conduct belong to the class or particular and ultimate things (since the prudent man admittedly has to take congizance of these things), and Understanding and Consideration deal with matters of conduct, which are ultimate. Also Intelligence apprehends the ultimates in both aspects--since ultimates as well as primary definitions {protos horos/syntagm} {horos} are grasped by intelligence {nous} and not reached by reasoning {logos}; in demonstrations {apodeixis} intelligence apprehends the immutable and primary definitions, in practical inferences {praktikais} the ultimate and contingent fact, and the minor premise, since these are the first principles from whcih the end is inferred, as general rules are based on particular cases; hence we must have perception of particulars, and this immediate perception is intelligence.

Εἰσὶ δὲ πᾶσαι αἱ ἕξεις εὐλόγως εἰς ταὐτὸ τείνουσαι· (25) λέγομεν γὰρ γνώμην καὶ σύνεσιν καὶ φρόνησιν καὶ νοῦν ἐπὶ τοὺς αὐτοὺς ἐπιφέροντες γνώμην ἔχειν καὶ νοῦν ἤδη καὶ φρονίμους καὶ συνετούς. πᾶσαι γὰρ αἱ δυνάμεις αὗται τῶν ἐσχάτων εἰσὶ καὶ τῶν καθ’ ἕκαστον· καὶ ἐν μὲν τῷ κριτικὸς εἶναι περὶ ὧν ὁ φρόνιμος, συνετὸς καὶ εὐγνώμων (30) ἢ συγγνώμων· τὰ γὰρ ἐπιεικῆ κοινὰ τῶν ἀγαθῶν ἁπάν- των ἐστὶν ἐν τῷ πρὸς ἄλλον. ἔστι δὲ τῶν καθ’ ἕκαστα καὶ τῶν ἐσχάτων ἅπαντα τὰ πρακτά· καὶ γὰρ τὸν φρόνιμον δεῖ γινώσκειν αὐτά, καὶ ἡ σύνεσις καὶ ἡ γνώμη περὶ τὰ πρακτά, ταῦτα δ’ ἔσχατα. καὶ ὁ νοῦς τῶν ἐσχάτων ἐπ’ (35) ἀμφότερα· καὶ γὰρ τῶν πρώτων ὅρων καὶ τῶν ἐσχάτων (1143b) νοῦς ἐστὶ καὶ οὐ λόγος, καὶ ὁ μὲν κατὰ τὰς ἀποδείξεις (1143b) νοῦς ἐστὶ καὶ οὐ λόγος, καὶ ὁ μὲν κατὰ τὰς ἀποδείξεις τῶν ἀκινήτων ὅρων καὶ πρώτων, ὁ δ’ ἐν ταῖς πρακτικαῖς τοῦ ἐσχάτου καὶ ἐνδεχομένου καὶ τῆς ἑτέρας προτάσεως· ἀρχαὶ γὰρ τοῦ οὗ ἕνεκα αὗται· ἐκ τῶν καθ’ ἕκαστα γὰρ τὰ καθόλου· τούτων οὖν ἔχειν δεῖ αἴσθησιν, αὕτη δ’ ἐστὶ νοῦς. @1 (5) διὸ καὶ φυσικὰ δοκεῖ εἶναι ταῦτα, καὶ φύσει σοφὸς μὲν οὐδείς, γνώμην δ’ ἔχειν καὶ σύνεσιν καὶ νοῦν. σημεῖον δ’ ὅτι καὶ ταῖς ἡλικίαις οἰόμεθα ἀκολουθεῖν, καὶ ἥδε ἡ ἡλικία νοῦν ἔχει καὶ γνώμην, ὡς τῆς φύσεως αἰτίας οὔσης. [διὸ καὶ ἀρχὴ καὶ τέλος νοῦς· ἐκ τούτων γὰρ αἱ ἀποδείξεις (10) καὶ περὶ τούτων.] ὥστε δεῖ προσέχειν τῶν ἐμπείρων καὶ πρεσβυτέρων ἢ φρονίμων ταῖς ἀναποδείκτοις φάσεσι καὶ δόξαις οὐχ ἧττον τῶν ἀποδείξεων· διὰ γὰρ τὸ ἔχειν ἐκ τῆς ἐμπειρίας ὄμμα ὁρῶσιν ὀρθῶς. τί μὲν οὖν ἐστὶν ἡ φρόνησις καὶ ἡ σοφία, καὶ περὶ τί ἑκατέρα τυγχάνει (15) οὖσα, καὶ ὅτι ἄλλου τῆς ψυχῆς μορίου ἀρετὴ ἑκατέρα, εἴρηται.

This is why it is thought that these qualities are a natural gift, and that a man is considerate, understanding, and intelligent by nature, though no one is a wise man by nature. That is so is indicated by our thinking of them as going with certain ages; we say that at such and such an age a man must have got intelligence {nous} and considerateness {gnōm/emacron}, which implies that they come from nature. 
[Hence intelligence is both a beginning and an end, for these things are both the starting-point and the subject matter of demonstration] 
Consequently, the unproved assertions and opinions of experiences and elderly people, or of prudent men, are as much deserving of attention as those which they support by proof; for experience has given them an eye for things, and so they see correctly.

<$ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,section@10,NicomacheanEthics@86.0,chapter@12,book@6,bekker@1143.2,PRIMARY@534.0 >
<@49.19@ "We have now discussed the nature and respective spheres of Prudence and Wisdom, and have shown that each is the virtue of a different part of the soul" 
Διαπορήσειε δ’ ἄν τις περὶ αὐτῶν τί χρήσιμοί εἰσιν.
ἡ μὲν γὰρ σοφία οὐδὲν θεωρήσει ἐξ ὧν ἔσται εὐδαίμων ἄνθρωπος (οὐδεμιᾶς γάρ ἐστι γενέσεως), ἡ δὲ φρόνησις τοῦτο (20) μὲν ἔχει, ἀλλὰ τίνος ἕνεκα δεῖ αὐτῆς 

We have not discussed the nature and respective spheres of prudence and wisdom, and have shown that each is the virtue of a different part of the soul 
But the further question may be raised. What is the use of these intellectual virtues? 
εἴπερ ἡ μὲν φρόμὲν ἔχει, ἀλλὰ τίνος ἕνεκα δεῖ αὐτῆς; εἴπερ ἡ μὲν φρό- νησίς ἐστιν ἡ περὶ τὰ δίκαια καὶ καλὰ καὶ ἀγαθὰ ἀν- θρώπῳ, ταῦτα δ’ ἐστὶν ἃ τοῦ ἀγαθοῦ ἐστὶν ἀνδρὸς πράττειν, οὐδὲν δὲ πρακτικώτεροι τῷ εἰδέναι αὐτά ἐσμεν, εἴπερ ἕξεις αἱ ἀρεταί εἰσιν, ὥσπερ οὐδὲ τὰ ὑγιεινὰ οὐδὲ τὰ εὐεκτικά, (25) ὅσα μὴ τῷ ποιεῖν ἀλλὰ τῷ ἀπὸ τῆς ἕξεως εἶναι λέγεται 

_εἴπερ ἡ μὲν φρόνησίς ἐστιν ἡ περὶ τὰ δίκαια καὶ καλὰ καὶ ἀγαθὰ ἀν- θρώπῳ, ταῦτα δ’ ἐστὶν ἃ τοῦ ἀγαθοῦ ἐστὶν ἀνδρὸς πράττειν, οὐδὲν δὲ πρακτικώτεροι τῷ εἰδέναι αὐτά ἐσμεν, εἴπερ ἕξεις αἱ ἀρεταί εἰσιν, ὥσπερ οὐδὲ τὰ ὑγιεινὰ οὐδὲ τὰ εὐεκτικά, (25) ὅσα μὴ τῷ ποιεῖν ἀλλὰ τῷ ἀπὸ τῆς ἕξεως εἶναι λέγεται· 
Wisdom does not consider the means to human happiness at all, for it does not ask how anything comes into existence. 
Prudence, it must be granted, does do this; but what do we need it for? seeing that it studies that which is just and noble and good for man, but these are the things that a good man does by nature. Knowing about them doesn't make us any more capable of doing them, since the virtues are qualities of character.

<$argument@1,nutrition,bekker@1144.1,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,argument@2,wisdom,NicomacheanEthics@90.0,happiness,PRIMARY@538.0,chapter@12,book@6,section@15 >
<@49.22@ ARGUMENTS 
{argument@1} wisdom and prudence as parts of the [intellect] are desirable in themselves.

{argument@1} wisdom and prudence as parts of the [intellect] are desirable in themselves.

1144a) πρῶτον μὲν οὖν λέγωμεν ὅτι καθ’ αὑτὰς ἀναγκαῖον αἱρετὰς αὐτὰς εἶναι, ἀρετάς γ’ οὔσας ἑκατέραν ἑκατέρου τοῦ μορίου, καὶ εἰ μὴ ποιοῦσι μηδὲν μηδετέρα αὐτῶν.

(2) {argument@2} they do in fact produce an an effect. {wisdom} produces {happiness}, but not in the sense in which medicine produces health, but in the sense in whcih healthiness is the cause of health.

ἔπειτα καὶ ποιοῦσι μέν, οὐχ ὡς ἡ ἰατρικὴ δὲ ὑγίειαν, ἀλλ’ ὡς ἡ ὑγίεια, οὕτως ἡ σοφία εὐδαιμονίαν· 
For wisdom is a part of virtue as a whole, and therefore by its possession, or rather by its exercise, renders a man happy.

μέρος γὰρ οὖσα τῆς ὅλης ἀρετῆς τῷ ἔχεσθαι ποιεῖ καὶ †τῷ ἐνεργεῖν εὐδαίμονα.† ἔτι τὸ ἔργον ἀποτελεῖται κατὰ τὴν φρόνησιν καὶ τὴν ἠθικὴν ἀρετήν· 
Also, prudence as well as moral virtue determines the complete performance of man's proper function: virtues ensures the rightness of the end we aim at, prudence ensures the rightness of the means we adopt to gain that end...
ἡ
μὲν γὰρ ἀρετὴ τὸν σκοπὸν ποιεῖ ὀρθόν, ἡ δὲ φρόνησις τὰ πρὸς τοῦτον. τοῦ δὲ τετάρτου μορίου τῆς ψυχῆς οὐκ ἔστιν ἀρετὴ τοιαύτη, τοῦ θρεπτικοῦ 
(The fourth part of the soul on the other hand, the nutritive faculty, has no virtue contributing to the proper function of man, since it has no power to act or not to act.) 
{nutrition}

<$bekker@1144.1,deinotēta,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,ethics,NicomacheanEthics@91.0,PRIMARY@539.0,chapter@12,book@6,section@15 >
<@49.23@ Going deeper into the object that prudence does not render men more capable of performing noble and just actions...

While some do just deeds but are not just men, there is a way of comporting oneself to do these things such that one is good.

, οὕτως, ὡς ἔοικεν, ἔστι τὸ πῶς ἔχοντα πράττειν ἕκαστα ὥστ’ εἶναι ἀγα- θόν, λέγω δ’ οἷον διὰ προαίρεσιν καὶ αὐτῶν ἕνεκα τῶν πραττομένων 
I mean when he does them from choice, and for the sake of the acts themselves.

λέγω δ’ οἷον διὰ προαίρεσιν καὶ αὐτῶν ἕνεκα τῶν πραττομένων. τὴν μὲν οὖν προαίρεσιν ὀρθὴν ποιεῖ ἡ ἀρετή, (20) τὸ δ’ ὅσα ἐκείνης ἕνεκα πέφυκε πράττεσθαι οὐκ ἔστι τῆς ἀρετῆς ἀλλ’ ἑτέρας δυνάμεως 
Now rightness in our choice of an end is secured by (moral) virtue; but to do the actions that must in the nature of things be done in order to attain the end we have chosen, is not a matter for virtue but for a different faculty. 
λεκτέον δ’ ἐπιστήσασι σα- φέστερον περὶ αὐτῶν. ἔστι δὴ δύναμις ἣν καλοῦσι δει- @1 νότητα· 
We must dwell on this point to make it more clear.

There is a certain faculty called cleverness {deinotēta} which is the capacity for doing the things aforesaid that conduce to the end we propose, and so attaining that end.

_αὕτη δ’ ἐστὶ τοιαύτη ὥστε τὰ πρὸς τὸν ὑποτεθέντα σκοπὸν συντείνοντα δύνασθαι ταῦτα πράττειν καὶ τυγχά-\νειν αὐτοῦ. ἂν μὲν οὖν ὁ σκοπὸς ᾖ καλός, ἐπαινετή ἐστιν, ἐὰν δὲ φαῦλος, πανουργία 
If the aim is noble, this is a praiseworthy faculty, but if base, it is mere knavery 

_ὥστε φανερὸν ὅτι ἀδύνατον φρόνιμον εἶναι μὴ ὄντα (1144b) ἀγαθόν 

_διὸ καὶ τοὺς φρονίμους δεινοὺς καὶ πανούργους φαμὲν εἶναι. 
this is how we come to speak of both prudent men and knaves as clever.

στι δ’ ἡ φρόνησις οὐχ ἡ δύ- ναμις, ἀλλ’ οὐκ ἄνευ τῆς δυνάμεως ταύτης.

Now this caulty is not identical with prudence, but prudence implies it.

ἡ δ’ ἕξις τῷ ὄμματι τούτῳ γίνεται τῆς ψυχῆς οὐκ ἄνευ ἀρετῆς, ὡς (30) εἴρηταί τε καὶ ἔστι δῆλον· 
But that eye of the soul [??] of which we spoke cannot acquire the quality of Prudence without possessing Virtue. This we have said before, for it is manifestly true.

_οἱ γὰρ συλλογισμοὶ τῶν πρα- κτῶν ἀρχὴν ἔχοντές εἰσιν, ἐπειδὴ τοιόνδε τὸ τέλος καὶ τὸ ἄριστον, ὁτιδήποτε ὄν (ἔστω γὰρ λόγου χάριν τὸ τυχόν)· 
For deductive inferences about matters of conduct always have a major premise of the form Since the End or Supreme good is so and so (whatever it may be, since we may take it as anything we like for the sake of argument) 
τοῦτο δ’ εἰ μὴ τῷ ἀγαθῷ, οὐ φαίνεται· 
but the supreme good only appears good to the good man; 
διαστρέφει γὰρ ἡ μοχθηρία καὶ διαψεύδεσθαι ποιεῖ περὶ τὰς πρακτικὰς ἀρ- (35) χάς. ὥστε φανερὸν ὅτι ἀδύνατον φρόνιμον εἶναι μὴ ὄντα (1144b) ἀγαθόν 
vice perverts the mind and causes it to hold false views about the first principles of conduct. Hence it is clear that we cannot be prudent without being good

<$PRIMARY@540.0,bekker@1144.2,chapter@13,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,book@6,NicomacheanEthics@92.0,kuria,authoritative virtue,natural virtue,section@15,Socrates >
<@49.24@ We have therefore also to reconsider the nature of Virtue.

Draws a distinction between {natural virtue} and {authoritative virtue} {kuria} 
{authoritative virtue} cannot exist without prudence 
{Socrates} said that all the virtues are prudence. This is wrong. It is correct however that virtues cannot exist without prudence.

"A proof of this is that everyone, even at the present day, in defining virtue , after saying what disposition it is and specifying the things with which it is concerned, adds that it is a disposition determined by the right principle; and the right principle is determined by prudence." 
σημεῖον δέ· καὶ γὰρ νῦν πάντες, ὅταν ὁρίζωνται τὴν ἀρετήν, προστιθέασι, τὴν ἕξιν εἰπόντες καὶ πρὸς ἅ ἐστι, τὴν κατὰ τὸν ὀρθὸν λόγον· ὀρθὸς δ’ ὁ κατὰ τὴν φρόνησιν. ἐοίκασι δὴ μαντεύεσθαί πως ἅπαντες ὅτι ἡ τοιαύτη ἕξις ἀρετή ἐστιν, ἡ κατὰ τὴν φρόνησιν.

It appears therefore that everybody in some sense divines that Virtue is a disposition of this nature, namely regulated by prudence. 
This formula however however requires a slight modification.

_δεῖ (25) δὲ μικρὸν μεταβῆναι.

Virtue is not merely a disposition conforming to right principle; but one co-operating with right principle; and prudence is right principle in matters of conduct 
ἔστι γὰρ οὐ μόνον ἡ κατὰ τὸν ὀρθὸν λόγον, ἀλλ’ ἡ μετὰ τοῦ ὀρθοῦ λόγου ἕξις ἀρετή ἐστιν· ὀρθὸς δὲ λόγος περὶ τῶν τοιούτων ἡ φρόνησίς ἐστιν.

Σωκράτης μὲν οὖν λόγους τὰς ἀρετὰς ᾤετο εἶναι (ἐπιστήμας γὰρ εἶναι 

^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-19 20:53:49.397764']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$NicomacheanEthics@93.0,bekker@1144.2,ethics,chapter@13,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,meta,PRIMARY@541.0,book@6,section@15,Socrates >
<@49.25@ {Socrates} then thought that the virtues are principles, for he said that they are all of them forms of knowledge. We on the other hand say that the virtues co-operate with principles.

_Σωκράτης μὲν οὖν λόγους τὰς ἀρετὰς ᾤετο εἶναι (ἐπιστήμας γὰρ εἶναι πάσας), ἡμεῖς δὲ μετὰ λόγου. δῆλον οὖν ἐκ τῶν εἰρημένων (30) ὅτι οὐχ οἷόν τε ἀγαθὸν εἶναι κυρίως ἄνευ φρονήσεως, οὐδὲ φρόνιμον ἄνευ τῆς ἠθικῆς ἀρετῆς. ἀλλὰ καὶ ὁ λόγος ταύτῃ λύοιτ’ ἄν, ᾧ διαλεχθείη τις ἂν ὅτι χωρίζονται ἀλ- λήλων αἱ ἀρεταί 
[the word {meta} is translated as co-operating: annoying!] 
These considerations therefore show that it is not possible to be good in the true sense without prudence, nor to be prudent without moral virtue.

<$NicomacheanEthics@94.0,chapter@13,ethics,Nicomachean Ethics/text,text@Nicomachean Ethics,bekker@1145.1,PRIMARY@542.0,book@6,section@15 >
<@49.26@ These considerations therefore show that it is not possible to be good in the authoritative sense without prudence, nor to be prudent without moral virtue.

δῆλον οὖν ἐκ τῶν εἰρημένων (30) ὅτι οὐχ οἷόν τε ἀγαθὸν εἶναι κυρίως ἄνευ φρονήσεως, οὐδὲ φρόνιμον ἄνευ τῆς ἠθικῆς ἀρετῆς.

If some one has one of the virtues of prudence, he will have them all... 
ῆλον δέ, κἂν εἰ μὴ πρακτικὴ ἦν, ὅτι ἔδει ἂν αὐτῆς διὰ τὸ τοῦ μορίου ἀρετὴν εἶναι, καὶ ὅτι οὐκ ἔσται ἡ προαίρεσις ὀρθὴ ἄνευ φρονήσεως οὐδ’ ἄνευ ἀρετῆς· ἣ μὲν γὰρ τὸ τέλος ἣ δὲ (5) τὰ πρὸς τὸ τέλος ποιεῖ πράττειν. ἀλλὰ μὴν οὐδὲ κυρία γ’ ἐστὶ τῆς σοφίας οὐδὲ τοῦ βελτίονος μορίου, ὥσπερ οὐδὲ @1 τῆς ὑγιείας ἡ ἰατρική· οὐ γὰρ χρῆται αὐτῇ, ἀλλ’ ὁρᾷ ὅπως γένηται· ἐκείνης οὖν ἕνεκα ἐπιτάττει, ἀλλ’ οὐκ ἐκείνῃ.
ἔτι ὅμοιον κἂν εἴ τις τὴν πολιτικὴν φαίη ἄρχειν τῶν θεῶν, (10) ὅτι ἐπιτάττει περὶ πάντα τὰ ἐν τῇ πόλει.

It is therefore clear that, even if prudence had no bearing on conduct, it would still be needed, because it is the virtue of that part of the intellect to which it belongs; and also that our choice of actions will not be right without prudence any more than without moral virtue, since, while moral virtue enables us to achieve the end, prudence makes us adopt the right means to the end.

But nevertheless it is not really the case that prudence is in authority over wisdom, or ovber the higher part of the intellect, any more than medical science is in authority over health.
Medical science does not control health, but studes how to procure it; hence it issues orders in the interests of health, but not to health. And again, one might as well say that political science governs the gods, because it gives orders about everything in the state.

<@59@ {DISTINCTION} The three modes of {persuasion} 
Now the proofs furnished by the sppech are of three kinds. The first depends upon the moral character of the speaker, the second upon putting the hearer into a certain frame of mind, the third upon the speech itsefl, in so far as it proves or seems to prove.

τῶν δὲ διὰ τοῦ λόγου ποριζομένων πίστεων τρία εἴδη ἔστιν· αἱ μὲν γάρ εἰσιν ἐν τῷ ἤθει τοῦ λέγοντος, αἱ δὲ ἐν τῷ τὸν ἀκροατὴν διαθεῖναί πως, αἱ δὲ ἐν αὐτῷ τῷ λόγῳ διὰ τοῦ δεικνύναι ἢ φαίνεσθαι δεικνύναι. διὰ μὲν οὖν τοῦ ἤθους, ὅταν οὕτω λεχθῇ ὁ λόγος ὥστε ἀξιό- (5) πιστον ποιῆσαι τὸν λέγοντα·

<$chapter@2,book@1,Rhetoric/text,DISTINCTION,enthymeme,bekker@1356.2,PRIMARY@62.0,rhetoric,induction,text@Rhetoric,argumentation,section@7,example,Rhetoric@13.0,syllogism >
<@64@ {DISTINCTION} modes of {argumentation} in {rhetoric} 
But for purposes of demonstration, real or apparent, just as dialectic possesses two modes of argument, induction and the syllogism, real or apparent, the same is the case in rhetoric.

For the {example} is {induction} and the {enthymeme} a {syllogism} 
τῶν δὲ διὰ τοῦ δει- (35) κνύναι ἢ φαίνεσθαι δεικνύναι, καθάπερ καὶ ἐν τοῖς δια- (1356b) λεκτικοῖς τὸ μὲν ἐπαγωγή ἐστιν, τὸ δὲ συλλογισμός, τὸ δὲ φαινόμενος συλλογισμός, καὶ ἐνταῦθα ὁμοίως· ἔστιν γὰρ τὸ μὲν παράδειγμα ἐπαγωγή, τὸ δ’ ἐνθύμημα συλλογισμός, τὸ δὲ φαι- νόμενον ἐνθύμημα φαινόμενος συλλογισμός

<@65@ {example} {enthyme} {compare@topics} 
The difference between example and enthymeme is evident from the {Topics/text} where in discussing {syllogism} and {induction}, it has previously been said that the proof from a number of particular cases that such is the rule,m is called in {dialectic} induction, in {rhetoric} example; but when, certain things being posited, something different results by reason of them, alongside of them, from their being true, either universally or in most cases, such a conclusion in dialectic is called a {syllogism}, in rhetoric an {enthymeme}

<$chapter@2,Rhetoric@19.0,necessity,book@1,bekker@1357.1,Rhetoric/text,PRIMARY@68.0,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,syllogism,section@14 >
<@70@ But since few of the propositions of the rhetorical {syllogism} are necessary {necessity} for most of the things which we judge and examine can be other than they are, human actions [ta prattomena] which are the subject of our deliberation and examination, being all of such a character, and, generally speaking, none of them necessary; since, further, facts which only generally happen or are merely possible can only be demonstrated by other facts of the same kind, and necessary facts by necessary propositions, it is evident that the materials from which enthymemes are derived will be sometimes necessary, but for the most part only generally true

<@71@ {probability} {DEFINITION} 
For that which is probable is that which generally happens, not however unreservedly, as some define it, but that which is concerned with things that may be other than they are, being so related to that in regard to which it is probably as the universal to the particular 
τὸ μὲν γὰρ εἰκός ἐστι τὸ ὡς ἐπὶ τὸ πολὺ γινόμενον, οὐχ ἁπλῶς δὲ καθάπερ ὁρίζονταί τινες, ἀλλὰ τὸ περὶ τὰ ἐν- (35) δεχόμενα ἄλλως ἔχειν, οὕτως ἔχον πρὸς ἐκεῖνο πρὸς ὃ εἰκὸς δεχόμενα ἄλλως ἔχειν, οὕτως ἔχον πρὸς ἐκεῖνο πρὸς ὃ εἰκὸς (1357b) ὡς τὸ καθόλου πρὸς τὸ κατὰ μέρος·

<$Rhetoric@85.0,PRIMARY@134.0,chapter@1,Rhetoric/text,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@2,bekker@1377.2,section@1 >
<@134@ But since the object of rhetoric is judgment--for judgments are pronounced in deliberative rhetoric and judicial proceedings are a judgment---it is not only necessary to consider how to make the speech itself demonstrative and convincing, but also that the speaker should show himself to be of a certain character and show know how to put the judge into a certain frame of mind.

For it makes a great difference with regard to producing conviction--especially in demonstrative and next to this in forensic oratory---that the speaker should show himself to be possessed of certain qualities and that his hearers should think that he is disposed in a certain way towards them; and further, that they themselves should be disposed in a certain way towards him

<$PRIMARY@136.0,chapter@1,Rhetoric/text,phainesthai/gr,rhetoric,project@Truth Book,REFLECTIONS@15.0,Rhetoric@87.0,book@2,text@Rhetoric,truth,Heidegger,diekeimai/gr,bekker@1377.2,section@1,REFLECTION >
<@134.1.1@ {REFLECTION} 
Rhetoric concerns how the speaker should show himself {phainesthai/gr} and how the hearer is disposed, and how he is disposed towards his hearers. {diekeimai/gr} 

This is very important for what I am trying to argue, since it suggests that there is a different kind of {truth} [as I use the term] at play in rhetoric; one which has to do with the coming into appearance in the field of the agora; what we might characterize as a limited attentional space... 
Ultimately: this attentional space is characterized by "care" -- as {Heidegger} would say...

<$section@4,oratory,Rhetoric/text,deliberative,bekker@1378.1,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@2,PRIMARY@137.0,Rhetoric@88.0,chapter@1 >
<@135@ In {deliberative} {oratory} it is more useful that the orator should appear of a certain character, in forensic; that the hearer should be disposed in a certain way; for opinions vary, according as men love or hate, are wrathful or mild, and things appear either altogether different, or different in degree; for when a man is favourably disposed towards one on whome he is passing judgment, he either things that that the accused has committed no wrong at all, or that his offence is trifling; but if he hates him, the reverse is the case

<$life world,bekker@1378.1,rhetoric,truth,PRIMARY@140.0,political,library,ekklesia,importance,agora,phenoemnology,project@Truth Book,disclosure,REFLECTION,REFLECTIONS@16.0,section@4,book@2,objects,chapter@1,Rhetoric/text,text@Rhetoric,assembly,Rhetoric@91.0 >
<@136.1@ {REFLECTION} 
THIS PASSAGE is extremely important for my reading of the Rhetoric, since it suggests the extent to which the intimations of a radical conception of the {life world} of {phenoemnology}, but with a specifically political sense, can be found in the Rhetoric.

This suggests a specifically {political} mode of {truth}, which is characterized not by the {disclosure} of the {objects} of properly scientific quality, but rather by the "appearing" of the objects of "care" according to the emotional disposition of the members of the community.

Of decisive significance here is the limited attentional space of the {agora} or {ekklesia} {assembly}. 
This is very different from the potentially infinite space of scientific truth...The model of which might be the {library}... 
While scientific truth is organized according to the {importance} of the thing itself, its primacy in the relation of causes and effects---hence God as the highest object of contemplation---the truth of the sphere of opinion, political truth as we might say (or political opinion---The terminology is indeed going to get rather confusing)---is organized according to "care"

^:size|I|60:^ >

<$Rhetoric@97.0,Rhetoric/text,bekker@1378.1,rhetoric,emotion,book@2,text@Rhetoric,PRIMARY@146.0,STRUCTURE,section@8,chapter@1,pathos >
<@140@ {STRUCTURE} {emotion} 
And each of them {pathos} may be deivided under three heads: for instance, in regard to anger, the disposition of mind which makes men angry, the person with whom they are usually angry, and the occasions which give rise to anger. For if we knew one or even two of these heads, but not all three, it would be impossible to arouse that emotion. The same applies to the rest.

εῖ δὲ διαιρεῖν περὶ ἕκαστον εἰς τρία, λέγω δ’ οἷον περὶ ὀργῆς πῶς τε διακείμενοι ὀργίλοι εἰσί, καὶ τίσιν εἰώθασιν ὀργίζεσθαι, καὶ ἐπὶ ποίοις· εἰ γὰρ τὸ μὲν ἓν ἢ τὰ δύο ἔχοιμεν τούτων, ἅπαντα δὲ μή, ἀδύνατον ἂν εἴη τὴν (25) ὀργὴν ἐμποιεῖν· ὁμοίως δὲ καὶ ἐπὶ τῶν ἄλλων. ὥσπερ οὖν καὶ ἐπὶ τῶν προειρημένων διεγράψαμεν τὰς προτάσεις, οὕτω καὶ περὶ τούτων ποιήσωμεν καὶ διέλωμεν τὸν εἰρημένον τρόπον.

<$chapter@2,phainesthai,DEFINITION,PRIMARY@147.0,Rhetoric/text,bekker@1378.1,Rhetoric@98.0,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@2,anger,section@1,REFLECTION >
<@142@ {anger} {DEFINITION} 
[my translation] Let anger be a desire with pain of an apparent revenge for an apparent slight concerning himself or others, with such a slight not being deserved.

[Loeb] Let us define anger as a longing, accompanied by pain, for a real or apparent revenge for a real or apparent slight, affecting a man himself or one of his friends, when such a slight is undeserved.

__Ἔστω δὴ ὀργὴ ὄρεξις μετὰ λύπης τιμωρίας [φαινομένης] (30) διὰ φαινομένην ὀλιγωρίαν εἰς αὐτὸν ἤ  τῶν αὐτοῦ, τοῦ ὀλιγωρεῖν μὴ προσήκοντος

<$chapter@2,bekker@1378.2,DEFINITION,oligōria/gr,section@3,Rhetoric/text,slighting,rhetoric,PRIMARY@149.0,text@Rhetoric,book@2,Rhetoric@100.0,REFLECTION >
<@144@ {slighting} {DEFINITION} {oligōria/gr} 
ἡ ὀλιγωρία ἐστὶν ἐνέργεια δόξης περὶ τὸ μηδενὸς ἄξιον φαι- νόμενον (καὶ γὰρ τὰ κακὰ καὶ τἀγαθὰ ἄξια οἰόμεθα σπουδῆς εἶναι, καὶ τὰ συντείνοντα πρὸς αὐτά· ὅσα δὲ μηδέν τι ἢ μικρόν, οὐδενὸς ἄξια ὑπολαμβάνομεν), τρία ἐστὶν εἴδη ὀλιγωρίας, καταφρόνησίς τε καὶ ἐπηρεασμὸς καὶ ὕβρις 
Slighting is the actualization of opinion in regard to something which appears valueless; for things which are really bad or good, or tend to become so, we consider worthy of attention, but those which are of no importance or trifling we ignore.

// {REFLECTION} 
AGAIN, suggests the phenomenological perspective

<@179@ {favor} {benevolence} 
The persons towards whom men feel benevolent, and for what reasons, and in what frame of mind, will be clear when we have defined what favour is. Let it then be taken to be the feeling in accordance with which one who has it is said to render a service to one who needs it, not in return for something nor in the interest of him who renders it, but in that of the recipient 
{charis/gr} 
Τίσιν δὲ χάριν ἔχουσι καὶ ἐπὶ τίσιν καὶ πῶς αὐτοὶ ἔχοντες, ὁρισαμένοις τὴν χάριν δῆλον ἔσται. ἔστω δὴ χάρις, καθ’ ἣν ὁ ἔχων λέγεται χάριν ἔχειν, ὑπουργία τῷ δεομένῳ μὴ ἀντί τινος, μηδ’ ἵνα τι αὐτῷ τῷ ὑπουργοῦντι ἀλλ’ ἵνα τι ἐκείνῳ· μεγάλη δὲ ἂν ᾖ σφόδρα δεόμενος, ἢ μεγάλων καὶ χαλεπῶν, ἢ ἐν καιροῖς (20) τοιούτοις, ἢ μόνος ἢ πρῶτος ἢ μάλιστα. δεήσεις δέ εἰσιν αἱ ὀρέξεις, καὶ τούτων μάλιστα αἱ μετὰ λύπης τοῦ μὴ γιγνομέ- νου.
{DEFINITION}

<$DEFINITION,Rhetoric@150.0,emulation,PRIMARY@199.0,Rhetoric/text,rhetoric,chapter@11,RELATION,book@2,envy,text@Rhetoric,bekker@1388.2,section@1 >
<@189@ {emulation} 
{DEFINITION} is a feeling of pain at the evident presence of highly valued goods, which are possible for us to obtain, in the possession of those who naturally resemble us---pain not due to the fact that another poesses them, but to the fact that we ourselves do not...

<$bekker@1389.1,ethos/gr,Rhetoric@152.0,Rhetoric/text,PRIMARY@201.0,chapter@12,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@2,character,section@1,TRANSITION >
<@191@ {TRANSITION} to the {character}s {ethos/gr} 
Let us now describe the nature of the characters of men according to their emotions, habits, ages, and fortune.

By the emotions I mean anger, desire, and the like, of which we have already spoken; by habits virtues and vices, of which also we have previously spoken, as well as the kind of things men individually and deliberately choose and practice. The ages are youth, the primne of life, and old age. By fortune I mean noble brith, wealth, power, anbd the contraries, and in general good or bad fortune.

τὰ δὲ ἤθη ποῖοί τινες κατὰ τὰ πάθη καὶ τὰς ἕξεις καὶ τὰς ἡλικίας καὶ τὰς τύχας, διέλθωμεν μετὰ ταῦτα. λέγω δὲ πάθη μὲν ὀργὴν ἐπιθυμίαν καὶ τὰ τοιαῦτα περὶ ὧν εἰρήκαμεν [πρό-ὲν ὀργὴν ἐπιθυμίαν καὶ τὰ τοιαῦτα περὶ ὧν εἰρήκαμεν [πρό- τερον], ἕξεις δὲ ἀρετὰς καὶ κακίας, εἴρηται δὲ περὶ τούτων πρότερον, καὶ ποῖα προαιροῦνται ἕκαστοι, καὶ ποίων πρακτι- (35) κοί. ἡλικίαι δέ εἰσι νεότης καὶ ἀκμὴ καὶ γῆρας. τύχην δὲ (1389a) λέγω εὐγένειαν καὶ πλοῦτον καὶ δυνάμεις καὶ τἀναντία τούτοις καὶ ὅλως εὐτυχίαν καὶ δυστυχίαν.

<$bekker@1395.2,PRIMARY@228.0,Rhetoric/text,enthymeme,rhetoric,Rhetoric@179.0,chapter@22,book@2,text@Rhetoric,section@1 >
<@218@ Let us now speak of {enthymeme}s in general and the manner of look for them, and next of their topics; for each of these things is different in kind.

We have already said that the enthymeme is a kind of syllogism, what makes it so, and in what it differs from the dialectic syllogisms; for the conclusion must neither be drawn from too far back, nor should it include all the steps of the argument. In the first case its length causes obscurity, in the second, it is simply a waste of words, because it states much that is obvious.

It is this that makes the ignorant more persuasive than the educated in the presence of crowds; as the poets say "the ignorant are more skilled at speaking before a mob"

<$bekker@1400.1,Rhetoric/text,belief,rhetoric,chapter@23,text@Rhetoric,book@2,Christianity,truth,Rhetoric@192.0,section@24,PRIMARY@241.0,REFLECTION >
<@231@ Another topic is derived from things which are thought to happen but are incredible, because it would never have been thought so, if they had not happened or almost happened. And further, these things are even more likely to be true; for we only believe in that which is, or that which is probably; if then a thing is incredible and not probably, it will be true; for it is not because it is probably and credible that we think it true. 
λλος ἐκ τῶν δοκούντων μὲν γίγνεσθαι ἀπίστων δέ, ὅτι (5) οὐκ ἂν ἔδοξαν, εἰ μὴ ἦν ἢ ἐγγὺς ἦν. καὶ ὅτι μᾶλλον· ἢ γὰρ τὰ ὄντα ἢ τὰ εἰκότα ὑπολαμβάνουσιν· εἰ οὖν ἄπιστον καὶ μὴ εἰκός, ἀληθὲς ἂν εἴη· οὐ γὰρ διά γε τὸ εἰκὸς καὶ πιθανὸν δοκεῖ οὕτως 
{truth} 
// {REFLECTION} 
VERY INTERESTING...The improbability of something becomes evidence for its truth, since it is believed to have happened, and yet is improbably, and hence would not have been believed if it had not happened 
{Christianity} based on this enthymeme? 

WHAT IS MOST INTERESTING HOWEVER, is that it seems to invert the relation between the {truth} and the {belief} -- at least as it is conceived by philosophy...{belief} becomes the ground for {truth} 

For those things which are simply plausible and commonsense, the plausibility is itself ground for belief, which is sustained by plausibility.

But this represents a kind of limit case of "belief" truth--- 

^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-11 19:25:29.621330']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$DIVISION,Rhetoric/text,enthymeme,Rhetoric@196.0,bekker@1401.1,fallasy,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@2,section@2,PRIMARY@245.0,chapter@24 >

<$DIVISION,fallacy,Rhetoric@197.0,Rhetoric/text,section@10,bekker@1401.1,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@2,PRIMARY@246.0,chapter@24 >
<@236@ {fallacy} CONTINUED --- {DIVISION} there are more than two!! 
3) Another fallacy consists in combining what is divided, or dividing what is combined 
4) Another is that of constructing or destroying by exaggeration, which takes place when the speaker, without having proved that any crime has actually been committed, exaggerates the supposed fact 
5) Another fallacy is that of the sign, for this argument also is illogical...For instance, if one were to say that those who love one another are useful to States, since the love of Harmodius and Aristogiton overthrew the tryant Hipparchus 
6) Another fallacy is derived from accident 
7) Another fallacy is that of consequence 
"For instance, in the ALexander (Paris) it is said that Paris is high-minded, because he despised the companionship of the common herd and dwelt on Ida by himself, for because the high-minded are of this character, Paris might also be high-minded" 
8) Another fallacy consists in taking what is not the cause for the cause, as when a thing has happened at the same time or after another 

9) Another fallacy is the ommision of when and how 
10) considering a thing first absolute, and then not absolutely, but only in a particular case. 
"For instance, in Dialectic, it is argued that that which is not *is*, for that which is not *is* that which is not; also, that the unknown can be known, for it can be known of the unknown that it is unknown" 
Similarly, in Rhetoric, an apparent enthymeme may arise from that which is not absolutely probably but only in particular cases. But this is not to be understood absolute...
Agathon: "One might perhaps say this very thing is probable, that many things happen to men that are not probably" 


^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-11 19:52:34.860819']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >
