<@255@ Further, metaphors must not be far-fetched, but we must give names to things that have none by deriving the metaphor from what is akin and of the same kind, so that, as soon as it is uttered, it is clearly seen to be akin, as in the famous enigma: 
I saw a man who glued bronze with fire upon another 

There was no name for what took place, but as in both cases there is a kind of application, he called the application of the cupping-glass guing.

<$Rhetoric@223.0,chapter@5,section@3,PRIMARY@272.0,DISTINCTION,bekker@1407.2,Rhetoric/text,purity,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,book@3,hellēnizein/gr >
<@263@ 
__Ὁ μὲν οὖν λόγος συντίθεται ἐκ τούτων, ἔστι δ’ ἀρχὴ τῆς λέξεως τὸ ἑλληνίζειν 
Translated: {hellēnizein/gr} translated as {purity} 
Such then are the elements of speech. But purity which is the foundation of style, depends upon five rules {DISTINCTION} 
1) connecting particles should be introduced in their natural order, before or after, as they require 
2) to employ special not generic terms 

3) avoiding ambiguous terms 

4) keeping the genders distinct 
5) observing number

<$Rhetoric/text,bekker@1408.1,PRIMARY@275.0,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,section@6,book@3,chapter@7,Rhetoric@226.0 >
<@268@ Appropriate style also makes the fact appear credible; for the mind of the hearer is impsoed upon under the impression that the speaker is speaking the truth, because, in such circumstances, his feelings are the same, so that he things that things are as he represents them; and the hearer always sympathizes with one who speaks emotionally, even though he really says nothing. This is why speakers oftern confound their hearers by mere noise.

<$chapter@10,Rhetoric/text,PRIMARY@282.0,Rhetoric@233.0,bekker@1410.2,rhetoric,text@Rhetoric,metaphor,metaphora/gr,book@3,section@1 >
<@274@ {metaphor} {metaphora/gr} 
Easy learning is naturally pleasant to all, and words mean something so that all words which make us learn something are most pleasant. 
Now, we do not know the meaning of strange words, and proper terms we know already. It is metaphor, therefore, that above all produces this effect; for when Homer calls old age stubble, he teaches and informs us through the genus; for both have lost their bloom 
· ἀρχὴ δ’ ἔστω ἡμῖν αὕτη. τὸ γὰρ μανθάνειν ῥᾳδίως ἡδὺ φύσει πᾶσιν ἐστί, τὰ δὲ ὀνόματα σημαίνει τι, ὥστε ὅσα τῶν ὀνομάτων (10)

<$PRIMARY@304.0,chapter@1,book@1,De Anima/text,bekker@402.1,text@On the Soul,soul,On the Soul/text,OnTheSoul@8.0,METHOD,section@1 >
<@290@ {METHOD} 
Perhaps our first business is to determine to which of the genera the soul belongs, and what it is; I mean whether it is a particular thing, i.e. a substance, or whether it is a quality, or quantity, or belongs to any of ther other preestablished categories, and furthermore whether it has potential or actual existence.

πρῶτον δ’ ἴσως ἀναγκαῖον διελεῖν ἐν τίνι τῶν γενῶν καὶ τί ἐστι, λέγω δὲ πότερον τόδε τι καὶ οὐσία ἢ ποιὸν ἢ ποσόν, ἢ καί τις ἄλλη τῶν διαιρεθεισῶν κατηγοριῶν, ἔτι δὲ πότερον τῶν ἐν (25) δυνάμει ὄντων ἢ μᾶλλον ἐντελέχειά τις· 

SECONDLY: whether it has parts or not, and if every soul is of the same kind. and if not, whether the difference is one of species or genus σκεπτέον δὲ καὶ εἰ μεριστὴ ἢ ἀμερής, καὶ πότερον ὁμοειδὴς ἅπασα ψυχὴ ἢ οὔ· εἰ δὲ μὴ ὁμοειδής, πότερον εἴδει διαφέρουσα ἢ γένει.

<$mathematics,metaphysics,natural philosophy,book@1,De Anima/text,natural philosopher,DISTINCTION,section@13,text@On the Soul,OnTheSoul@20.0,soul,bekker@403.2,first philosophy,On the Soul/text,pathē/gr,chapter@1,PRIMARY@316.0 >
<@301@ The {natural philosopher}'s concern is with all the fuinctions and affections of a given body, i.e. of matter in a given state; any attribute not of this kind is the business of another; in some subjects it is the business of the expert, the carpenter, it may be, or the physician; but inseperables in so far as they are not affections of the body in such a state, that is, in the abstract, are the province of the mathematician, and in so far as they are separable they are the sphere of the first philosopher.

ὁ φυσικὸς περὶ ἅπανθ’ ὅσα τοῦ τοιουδὶ σώματος καὶ τῆς τοι- @1 αύτης ὕλης ἔργα καὶ πάθη, ὅσα δὲ μὴ τοιαῦτα, ἄλλος, καὶ περὶ τινῶν μὲν τεχνίτης, ἐὰν τύχῃ, οἷον τέκτων ἢ ἰατρός, τῶν δὲ μὴ χωριστῶν μέν, ᾗ δὲ μὴ τοιούτου σώμα- τος πάθη καὶ ἐξ ἀφαιρέσεως, ὁ μαθηματικός, ᾗ δὲ κεχωρι- (15) σμένα, ὁ πρῶτος φιλόσοφος; ἀλλ’ ἐπανιτέον ὅθεν ὁ λόγος.
ἐλέγομεν δὴ ὅτι τὰ πάθη τῆς ψυχῆς οὕτως ἀχώριστα τῆς φυσικῆς ὕλης τῶν ζῴων, ᾗ γε τοιαῦθ’ ὑπάρχει  θυμὸς καὶ φόβος, καὶ οὐχ ὥσπερ γραμμὴ καὶ ἐπίπεδον.

{DISTINCTION} {natural philosophy} {metaphysics} {first philosophy} {mathematics} 
NATURAL PHILOSOPHER --- formed matter; a body being of such a kind; the works and affectiations {pathē/gr} of matter of such a kind.

MATHEMATICIAN --- inseparables in abstraction.

METAPHYSICIAN/FIRST PHILOSOPHER --- inseparables insofar as they are separable

<@303@ {Democritus/philosopher} 
{DOXOLOGY} 
The latter actually identified soul and mind; for he believed that truth is subjective. Hence he regards Homer's description in his swooning as "lying thinking other thoughts" as accurate [i.e. his delusions are true] He does not then employ the term mind as denoting a faculty concerned with the truth, but identifies the soul and the mind.

οὐ μὴν παντελῶς γ’ ὥσπερ Δημόκριτος. ἐκεῖνος μὲν γὰρ ἁπλῶς ταὐτὸν ψυχὴν καὶ νοῦν (τὸ γὰρ ἀληθὲς εἶ- ναι τὸ φαινόμενον, διὸ καλῶς ποιῆσαι [τὸν] Ὅμηρον ὡς ὁ Ἕκτωρ "κεῖτ’ ἀλλοφρονέων"· οὐ δὴ χρῆται τῷ νῷ ὡς δυνάμει (30) τινὶ περὶ τὴν ἀλήθειαν, ἀλλὰ ταὐτὸ λέγει ψυχὴν καὶ νοῦν)

<$sunthesis/gr,PRIMARY@320.0,304.6,bekker@430.2,De Anima/text,chapter@6,combination,falsehood,text@On the Soul,soul,truth,Empedocles/presocratic philosopher,book@3,OnTheSoul@24.0,On the Soul/text,section@1 >
<@304@ {truth} 
The thinking of indivisible objects of thought occurs among things concerning which there can be no falsehood; where truth and falsehood are possible there is implied a compounding of thoughts into a fresh unity, as {Empedocles/presocratic philosopher=philosopher} "where without necks the heads of many grew" and then were joined together by love...so also these separate entities are combined, as for instance incommensurable and diagonal.

But if the thinking is concerned with things past or future, then we take into account and include the notion of time. For {falsehood} always lies in the process of {combination} 
Ἡ μὲν οὖν τῶν ἀδιαιρέτων νόησις ἐν τούτοις περὶ ἃ οὐκ ἔστι τὸ ψεῦδος, ἐν οἷς δὲ καὶ τὸ ψεῦδος καὶ τὸ ἀληθές σύν- θεσίς τις ἤδη νοημάτων ὥσπερ ἓν ὄντων-καθάπερ Ἐμπεδο- κλῆς ἔφη "ᾗ πολλῶν μὲν κόρσαι ἀναύχενες ἐβλάστησαν", ἔπειτα συντίθεσθαι τῇ φιλίᾳ, οὕτω καὶ ταῦτα κεχωρισμένα (30) συντίθεται, οἷον τὸ ἀσύμμετρον καὶ ἡ διάμετρος-ἂν δὲ γενο- (430b) μένων ἢ ἐσομένων, τὸν χρόνον προσεννοῶν [καὶ] συντίθησι. τὸ γὰρ ψεῦδος ἐν συνθέσει ἀεί· 
{sunthesis/gr} 

^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-12 13:42:52.860886', '2021-03-12 13:42:58.714260', '2021-03-22 07:42:24.185351', '2021-03-22 07:43:08.747252', '2021-03-22 07:43:39.452718']:^

<$title@Aristotle's De anima,De Anima/text,page@408,PRIMARY@331.0,soul,type@book,OnTheSoul@35.0,author@Ronald Polansky,On the Soul/text,SECONDARY@1.0 >
<@304.1@ 
But why should Aristotle say that proper perception is "always true" ( 
_$% , 427b11-12)? Plato’s Theaetetus 186c-e denies that sense gets to truth at all; only thought can get to truth. Previously in 418a11-12 Aristotle claimed proper perception cannot be deceived ([ ( $ ), while now he insists that it is true. Its truth cannot consist in making judgments and getting to essence, as thought may. Sense instead captures what is to be perceived, the truth or reality of the sensible object. Sense accurately discriminates what is there to be discriminated because the sense and its object, as stated in 425b26-27, are in actuality the same.
What the sense actually perceives is what there is under the present conditions of the object, medium, and organ. And he can say that it always gets the truth not because it is never inaccurate (cf. 428b18-19) but because as a class the proper senses as available to all the animals are authoritative in their fields. There is no criterion for them beyond themselves, whereas thought can be checked by sense perception. If we seem to perceive something poorly - and a proper sense can do this when we are too far away, distracted, sick, and so on - the same sense in a better condition has to reassess what is perceived. Moreover, even misperception need not be or occasion a false judgment.

{page@408}

<@304.2@ {Averroes} In his {Long Commentary on the De Anima/title} {page@364} claims that "apprehension of indivisible things" involves neither the {true} nor the {false} 

"That is, apprehending simple incomposite things will be through intelligibles which are neither false nor true, which is called conceptualizaiton, while apprending composite things by [intellect] will be by virtue of intelligiible in which there is falsity and truth. He was content with the first division without the second, since opposite is understood through its opposite"

<$PRIMARY@334.0,bekker@320.2,commentaryof@On the Soul,Paraphrase of de Anima/text,author@Themistius,translator@Frederic M Schroeder,COMMENTARY@18.0,De Anima/text,page@117,Themistius/philosopher,OnTheSoul@38.0,text@On the Soul,compdate@350,soul,title@Paraphrase of de Anima,book@3,On the Soul/text >
<@304.4@ {Themistius/philosopher} 
{Paraphrase of de Anima/text} 
109.4 (430a26-31): So when this potential intellect acquires its own form through the productive intellect illuminating it,176 it first thinks the uncombined and undivided things that are signified (the ones distinguished in the categories), in which there is not yet truth and falsity. But as it progresses177 it also combines them with one another (e.g., "Socrates" with "walks"), and in these truth and falsity is indeed present. And it does not combine them like a heap, but so as to make the many one again, and to embrace in one act of thought the plurality of uncombined things that are signified. "Socrates does philosophy," that is, is like this, and this combination of thoughts resembles the combination of limbs in em pedo cles178 by which Love, for him, combines the scattered limbs of animals. And just as for him not every combination of limbs produces an animal, so neither in this case does every combination [produce] truth, but one type produces truth, another falsity (e.g., the combination of the incommensurable with the diagonal produces truth, but that of the commensurable falsity). And just as in his case the animal duly emerges uncombined out of uncombined limbs, so does the statement duly emerge uncombined out of uncombined things that are signified.17

<$author@Themistius,bekker@320.2,PRIMARY@335.0,commentaryof@On the Soul,translator@Frederic M Schroeder,De Anima/text,OnTheSoul@39.0,text@On the Soul,compdate@350,soul,COMMENTARY@19.0,title@Paraphrase of de Anima,book@3,page@124,On the Soul/text >
<@304.5@ 112.8 (430b26-27): And truth in its case is without qualification; indeed it is itself truth, as it does not express truth by thinking something else but by thinking itself. Our intellect, on the other hand, insofar as it has a slight resemblance195 to that intellect, does not also display truth without qualification, but [only] as what is contrary to falsity. For if truth lies in assertion, falsity must ie in denial, so that [our intellect] always has falsity confounded196 with truth, and thought about along with it.
112.14 (430b27-30): When, however, [our intellect] examines one of the uncombined things that are signified,197 and thinks about its form and the definition of its essence, it errs least, though it does not always light upon what is [true]. Instead like vision, which errs least in judging only the white thing, but errs frequently in asserting that the white thing is Cleon,198 [our] intellect is also in most cases unerring and correct as long as it restricts itself to just the thought of the essence (e.g., to that of the good or of the fine). But when it says that one thing is good and another fine, it frequently makes the wrong assignment,199 and transfers such qualities to things that are not of that sort,200 and in just such cases there is considerable falsity and error. It ought, then, to restrict itself to the forms and above all seek out those without matter,201 as the special function of the intellect concerns these.202

<$PRIMARY@321.0,OnTheSoul@25.0,bekker@430.2,De Anima/text,chapter@6,section@27,text@On the Soul,soul,truth,book@3,On the Soul/text >
<@305@ the thinking of the definition in the sense of the essence is always true and is not an instance of predication; but just as while the seeing of a proper object is always true, the judgment whether the white object is a man or not is not always true, so it is with every object abstracted from its matter.

ἔστι δ’ ἡ μὲν φάσις τι κατά τινος, ὥσπερ καὶ ἡ ἀπόφασις, καὶ ἀληθὴς ἢ ψευδὴς πᾶσα· ὁ δὲ νοῦς οὐ πᾶς, ἀπόφασις, καὶ ἀληθὴς ἢ ψευδὴς πᾶσα· ὁ δὲ νοῦς οὐ πᾶς, ἀλλ’ ὁ τοῦ τί ἐστι κατὰ τὸ τί ἦν εἶναι ἀληθής, καὶ οὐ τὶ κατά @1 τινος· ἀλλ’ ὥσπερ τὸ ὁρᾶν †τοῦ ἰδίου ἀληθές, εἰ δ’ ἄνθρωπος τὸ λευκὸν† ἢ μή, οὐκ ἀληθὲς ἀεί, οὕτως ἔχει ὅσα ἄνευ ὕλης.

{truth}

(1) sensing of the proper object ...always true 
(2) predicative judgment about an object ...true OR false 
(3) thinking of the what-it-is-to-be of something (its being-what-it-is, rather than its mere whatness?) ...always true

<@307@ Sensation, then, is like mere assertion and  thinking; when an object is pleasant or unpleasant,  the soul pursues or avoids it, thereby making  a sort of assertion or negation. To feel pleasure  or pain is to adopt an attitude with the sensitive  mean towards good or bad as such. This is what  avoidance or appetite, when actual, really  means, and the faculties of appetite or avoidance  are not really different from each other, or  from the sensitive faculty, though their essence  is different. 

τὸ μὲν οὖν αἰσθάνεσθαι ὅμοιον τῷ φάναι μόνον καὶ νοεῖν· ὅταν δὲ ἡδὺ ἢ λυπηρόν, οἷον καταφᾶσα ἢ ἀποφᾶσα διώκει ἢ φεύγει· καὶ ἔστι τὸ ἥδεσθαι καὶ λυπεῖσθαι τὸ ἐνεργεῖν    (10) τῇ αἰσθητικῇ μεσότητι πρὸς τὸ ἀγαθὸν ἢ κακόν, ᾗ τοιαῦτα. καὶ ἡ φυγὴ δὲ καὶ ἡ ὄρεξις ταὐτό, ἡ κατ’ ἐνέργειαν, καὶ οὐχ ἕτερον τὸ ὀρεκτικὸν καὶ τὸ φευκτικόν, οὔτ’ ἀλλήλων οὔτε τοῦ αἰσθητικοῦ· ἀλλὰ τὸ εἶναι ἄλλο.

<$320,metaphysics,chapter@1,Metaphysics@3.0,Metaphysics/text,bekker@1001.1,book@4,PRIMARY@339.0,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314@ METAPHYSICS BOOK GAMMA (IV) 

This concerns the science which studies "Being qua Being" 


^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-13 09:03:47.060200', '2021-03-15 12:34:22.130080']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$PRIMARY@340.0,metaphysics,PRIMARY@341.0,chapter@1,Metaphysics@6.0,being,INTRODUCTION,Metaphysics/text,Metaphysics@4.0,PRIMARY@342.0,Metaphysics@5.0,bekker@1001.1,book@4,being as being,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.1@ [SINCE THE FOLLOWING NOTES WILL ALL BE ON METAPHYSICS GAMMA, I'LL ORGANIZE THEM SLIGHTLY DIFFERENTLY, AS SUCCESSORS OF A SINGLE CHILD NOTE] 
Ἔστιν ἐπιστήμη τις ἣ θεωρεῖ τὸ ὂν ᾗ ὂν καὶ τὰ τούτῳ (21) ὑπάρχοντα καθ’ αὑτό. αὕτη δ’ ἐστὶν οὐδεμιᾷ τῶν ἐν μέρει λεγομένων ἡ αὐτή· οὐδεμία γὰρ τῶν ἄλλων ἐπισκοπεῖ καθόλου περὶ τοῦ ὄντος ᾗ ὄν, ἀλλὰ μέρος αὐτοῦ τι ἀποτε- μόμεναι περὶ τούτου θεωροῦσι τὸ συμβεβηκός, οἷον αἱ μαθη- (25) ματικαὶ τῶν ἐπιστημῶν. ἐπεὶ δὲ τὰς ἀρχὰς καὶ τὰς ἀκρο- τάτας αἰτίας ζητοῦμεν, δῆλον ὡς φύσεώς τινος αὐτὰς ἀναγκαῖον εἶναι καθ’ αὑτήν. εἰ οὖν καὶ οἱ τὰ στοιχεῖα τῶν ὄντων ζητοῦντες ταύτας τὰς ἀρχὰς ἐζήτουν, ἀνάγκη καὶ τὰ στοιχεῖα τοῦ ὄντος εἶναι μὴ κατὰ συμβεβηκὸς ἀλλ’ ᾗ (30) ὄν· διὸ καὶ ἡμῖν τοῦ ὄντος ᾗ ὂν τὰς πρώτας αἰτίας ληπτέον.

There is some science which theorizes the being as being and the things belonging to this with respect to itself. 
This is the same as none of those speaking in a partial way. 
None of the other [sciences] universally concerning the being *as* being, but cutting off some part of it theorize concerning this the accidental, such as the mathematical sciences 
But since we seek the beginnings and the highest causes, it is clear that these are of some nature with respect to itself. 
If therefore also those seeking the elements of the beings sought these same beginnings, it is necessary also that the elements be of the being not accidentally but *as* being. 
Therefore also the first causes of the being *as* being are to be accepted by us.

[My translation] 

{INTRODUCTION} 
{being} {being as being} 
[TRANSLATION of Sachs] 
There is a kind of knowledge that contemplates what is insofar as it is, and what belongs to it in its own right. And this is not the same as any of those that are spoken of as partial, since none of the other kinds of knowledge examines universally what pertains to being as being, but cutting off some part of it, they consider this attribute, as do the mathematical kinds of knowledge.. But since we aer seeking the sources and highest causes, it is clear that they must belong to some nature in its own right. So if also those who were weeking the elements of beings were in quest of these sources, the elements too must belong to what is, not incidentally but insofar as it is. For this reason, for us too it is the first causes of being as being that must be gotten hold of.

<@314.2@ [chapter 2] 
Τὸ δὲ ὂν λέγεται μὲν πολλαχῶς, ἀλλὰ πρὸς ἓν καὶ μίαν τινὰ φύσιν καὶ οὐχ ὁμωνύμως ἀλλ’ ὥσπερ καὶ τὸ ὑγιεινὸν ἅπαν πρὸς ὑγίειαν, τὸ μὲν τῷ φυλάττειν τὸ δὲ (35) τῷ ποιεῖν τὸ δὲ τῷ σημεῖον εἶναι τῆς ὑγιείας τὸ δ’ ὅτι (1003b) δεκτικὸν αὐτῆς, καὶ τὸ ἰατρικὸν πρὸς ἰατρικήν (τὸ μὲν @1 γὰρ τῷ ἔχειν ἰατρικὴν λέγεται ἰατρικὸν τὸ δὲ τῷ εὐφυὲς εἶναι πρὸς αὐτὴν τὸ δὲ τῷ ἔργον εἶναι τῆς ἰατρικῆς), ὁμοιοτρόπως δὲ καὶ ἄλλα ληψόμεθα λεγόμενα τούτοις, - οὕτω δὲ καὶ τὸ ὂν λέγεται πολλαχῶς μὲν ἀλλ’ ἅπαν (5) πρὸς μίαν ἀρχήν· 
Being is said in many ways, but with a view [pros] one thing and some one nature and not homonymously. 
Comparison with: {medicine} {analogy} 
οὕτω δὲ καὶ τὸ ὂν λέγεται πολλαχῶς μὲν ἀλλ’ ἅπαν (5) πρὸς μίαν ἀρχήν· 
Thus also the {being} is spoken in many ways but with a view to one source {archē/gr}

<$chapter@2,metaphysics,PRIMARY@344.0,bekker@1003.2,being,Metaphysics/text,book@4,section@7,nothingness,Metaphysics@8.0,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.3@ SUMMARIZES the different ways in which {being} is spoken 

_τὰ μὲν γὰρ ὅτι οὐσίαι, ὄντα λέγεται, τὰ δ’ ὅτι πάθη οὐσίας, τὰ δ’ ὅτι ὁδὸς εἰς οὐσίαν ἢ φθοραὶ ἢ στερήσεις ἢ ποιότητες ἢ ποιητικὰ ἢ γεννητικὰ οὐσίας ἢ τῶν πρὸς τὴν οὐσίαν λεγομένων, ἢ τούτων τινὸς ἀποφάσεις ἢ οὐσίας 
For on the one hand, these, that they are essences, are called beings, these that they are affectations of essence, these that they are a way into essence, or destructions, or deprivations, or producers, or productions, or things-having-become of essence, of some denial of certain of these or of substance 

_διὸ καὶ τὸ μὴ ὂν εἶναι μὴ ὄν φαμεν.

Therefor also the not being we same to be not being.
{nothingness} 

καθάπερ οὖν καὶ τῶν ὑγιεινῶν ἁπάντων μία ἐπιστήμη ἔστιν, ὁμοίως τοῦτο καὶ ἐπὶ τῶν ἄλλων 

Even as of all things involving health there is one science, this is likewise for the other sciences 
οὐ γὰρ μόνον τῶν καθ’ ἓν λεγομένων ἐπιστήμης ἐστὶ θεωρῆσαι μιᾶς ἀλλὰ καὶ τῶν πρὸς μίαν λεγομένων φύσιν· καὶ γὰρ ταῦτα τρόπον τινὰ λέγονται καθ’ ἕν. 
For it is not only of the things being spoken with respect to one thing that the theorizing belongs to one science but also concerning those things being spoken with a view to one nature

<$chapter@2,PRIMARY@345.0,metaphysics,kuriō/gr,bekker@1003.2,section@17,Metaphysics/text,book@4,Metaphysics@9.0,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.4@ αὶ γὰρ ταῦτα τρόπον τινὰ λέγονται καθ’ ἕν. δῆλον οὖν ὅτι καὶ τὰ ὄντα μιᾶς θεωρῆσαι (15) ᾗ ὄντα. πανταχοῦ δὲ κυρίως τοῦ πρώτου ἡ ἐπιστήμη, καὶ ἐξ οὗ τὰ ἄλλα ἤρτηται, καὶ δι’ ὃ λέγονται 
For these things also are spoken with a view toward one thing. 
Therefore it is evident that the theorizing of beings *as* beings belongs to one science 
For alltogether authoritative {kuriō/gr} is the science of what is first, and from which other things are hung, and through which they are spoken

<$chapter@2,metaphysics,section@20,bekker@1003.2,PRIMARY@347.0,Metaphysics@11.0,aisthē/sis,Metaphysics/text,phonē/gr,book@4,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.6@ ἅπαντος δὲ γένους καὶ αἴσθησις μία ἑνὸς καὶ ἐπιστήμη, οἷον γραμματικὴ μία οὖσα πάσας θεωρεῖ (20) τὰς φωνάς· 
Even as of every genos there is one perception {aisth/emacrongr} 

ιὸ καὶ τοῦ ὄντος ᾗ ὂν ὅσα εἴδη θεωρῆσαι μιᾶς ἐστὶν ἐπιστήμης τῷ γένει, τά τε εἴδη τῶν εἰδῶν. 

διὸ καὶ τοῦ ὄντος ᾗ ὂν ὅσα εἴδη θεωρῆσαι μιᾶς ἐστὶν ἐπιστήμης τῷ γένει, τά τε εἴδη τῶν εἰδῶν. 
Therefore also it belongs a science that is one in genus to theorize so many as there of forms of being *as* being, and the species of the science to study their species.

[SACHS]= Now of every calss that is one there is both one perception, and one kind of knowledge, as the grammatical art, which is one, considers all utterances, and for this reason it also belongs to a kind of knowledge that is generically one to study as many forms as there are of being as being, and to the species of it to study their species.

<$chapter@2,PRIMARY@349.0,metaphysics,en/gr,on/gr,bekker@1003.2,sameness,being,section@30,Metaphysics/text,Metaphysics@12.0,book@4,unity,PRIMARY@348.0,similarity,Metaphysics@13.0,oneness,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.7@ REGARDING the relation of {oneness} {unity}    {en/gr} and {being} {on/gr}    
εἰ δὴ τὸ ὂν καὶ τὸ ἓν ταὐτὸν καὶ μία φύσις    τῷ ἀκολουθεῖν ἀλλή- λοις ὥσπερ ἀρχὴ καὶ αἴτιον,    ἀλλ’ οὐχ ὡς ἑνὶ λόγῳ δηλού- μενα (διαφέρει    δὲ οὐθὲν οὐδ’ ἂν ὁμοίως ὑπολάβωμεν, ἀλλὰ  (25)   καὶ πρὸ ἔργου μᾶλλον)· ταὐτὸ γὰρ εἷς  ἄνθρωπος   καὶ ἄνθρωπος, καὶ ὢν ἄνθρωπος καὶ  ἄνθρωπος,   καὶ οὐχ ἕτερόν τι δηλοῖ κατὰ τὴν  λέξιν ἐπαναδιπλούμενον   τὸ εἷς ἄνθρωπος καὶ  εἷς ὢν ἄνθρωπος (δῆλον   δ’ ὅτι οὐ χωρίζεται  οὔτ’ ἐπὶ γενέσεως οὔτ’   ἐπὶ φθορᾶς), ὁμοίως  δὲ καὶ ἐπὶ τοῦ ἑνός, ὥστε   φανερὸν ὅτι @1  (30) ἡ πρόσθεσις ἐν τούτοις   ταὐτὸ δηλοῖ,  καὶ οὐδὲν ἕτερον τὸ ἓν παρὰ τὸ   ὄν, ἔτι δ’  ἡ ἑκάστου οὐσία ἕν ἐστιν οὐ κατὰ   συμβε- βηκός,  ὁμοίως δὲ καὶ ὅπερ ὄν τι·     
ὥσθ’ ὅσα περ τοῦ ἑνὸς εἴδη, τοσαῦτα καὶ τοῦ   ὄντος· περὶ ὧν τὸ τί ἐστι τῆς αὐτῆς ἐπιστήμης   τῷ γένει θεωρῆσαι, λέγω δ’ οἷον περὶ   (35)   ταὐτοῦ καὶ ὁμοίου καὶ τῶν ἄλλων τῶν τοιούτων.   σχεδὸν δὲ (1004a) πάντα ἀνάγεται τἀναντία  εἰς  τὴν ἀρχὴν ταύτην·   
 τεθεω-(1004a) πάντα ἀνάγεται τἀναντία εἰς  τὴν ἀρχὴν ταύτην· τεθεω- ρήσθω δ’ ἡμῖν ταῦτα  ἐν τῇ ἐκλογῇ τῶν ἐναντίων.   
[Sachs] But if being and oneness are one and    the same nature in that they follow upon  one   another, as do source and cause, thought  not   as being revealed in a single articulation    (though it makes no difference even if we   do  understand them alike, but is even more   convenient)---for  "one human bieng" and "a   human being that is"  and "a human being"  are  the same thing, and  nothing different  is revealed  by the redoubled  statement that  the human  being is one or that  the human  being that  is one *is* and it is  clear that  they are  distinct with respect to  either  coming into  being or passing away and  similarly  with oneness,  so that obviously the  addition  in these statements  signifies the  same thing,  and what is one  is nothing different  aside  from whats, while  further the thinghood  of  each thing is not  incidental but is likewise   the very thing  that something is--therefore,   just as many  forms as there are of oneness,   so many also  are there of being. It belongs   to a kind of  knowledge that is generically   the same to  consider what pertains to what   there are;  I mean, for example, about sameness   and similarity  and other such things. And  just  about all  opposites lead back to this  starting  point;  but let these have been examined  by  us in  the passages about contraries.    






// Since the being and one are of the same nature in so far as they are reciprocally consequent, even though they differ in definition, the consideration of {sameness} {similarity} also belongs to the same science as the consideration of being

Has the sense of "cleave","cling to", "come next to", "folow closely" 

{LSJ} 
C. Med., hold oneself fast, cling closely, τῷ προσφὺς ἐχόμην Od. 12.433, cf. Il.1.513, etc.; πρὸς ἀλλήλῃσιν Od.5.329: mostly c. gen., hold on by, cling to, [πέτρης] ib.429; χερσὶν ἀώτου 9.435; βρετέων A. Th.98 (lyr.); ἑξόμεσθά σου Ar.Pl.101; τῆς πληγῆς ἔχεται claps his hand on the place struck, D.4.40.
2. metaph., cleave, cling to, ἔργου Hdt. 8.11, X.HG7.2.19; γεωργίας BGU7.6 (iii A.D.); τῶν πραγμάτων Jul. Or.1.19a; βιοτᾶς, ἐλπίδος, E.Ion491, Fr.409; τῆς αὐτῆς γνώμης Th.1.140; lay hold on, take advantage of, τῶν ἀγαθῶν ἔχεο Thgn.32; προφάσιος ἔχεσθαι Hdt.6.94; fasten upon, attack, D.18.79; lay claim to, ἀμφοτέρων τῶν ἐπωνυμιέων Hdt.2.17; to be zealous for, [μάχης] S.OC 424; ἀληθείας Pl.Lg.709c; κοινῇ τῆς σωτηρίας X.An.6.3.17, etc.

<$philospohy,truth,Metaphysics@21.0,bios,PRIMARY@357.0,text@Metaphysics,sophistry,DISTINCTION,gnōristikos,chapter@2,metaphysics,section@30,bekker@1004.1,Metaphysics/text,dialectic,philosophy,RELATION,book@4,peirastikos >
<@314.13@ 
_ἐστὶ περὶ ὧν τοῦ φιλοσόφου ἐπισκέψασθαι @1 τὸ ἀληθές.

The task of the philosopher is to investigate that which belongs to being 
{truth} 
σημεῖον δέ· οἱ γὰρ διαλεκτικοὶ καὶ σοφισταὶ τὸ αὐτὸ μὲν ὑποδύονται σχῆμα τῷ φιλοσόφῳ· ἡ γὰρ σο- φιστικὴ φαινομένη μόνον σοφία ἐστί, καὶ οἱ διαλεκτικοὶ διαλέγονται περὶ ἁπάντων, κοινὸν δὲ πᾶσι τὸ ὄν ἐστιν, (20) διαλέγονται δὲ περὶ τούτων δῆλον ὅτι διὰ τὸ τῆς φιλοσοδιαλέγονται δὲ περὶ τούτων δῆλον ὅτι διὰ τὸ τῆς φιλοσο- φίας ταῦτα εἶναι οἰκεῖα. 
[SACHS] Here is a sign of this: those who engage in dialectic and the sophists slip into the same outward appearance as the philosopher. For sophistry is wisdom in appearance only, while dialectic discourses about everything, being is common to all things, so it is clear that they discourse about these topics just because they are proper to philosophy; for sophistry and dialectic trun themselves to the same class of things as philosophy, but it differes from one of them in the way its power is turned, and from the other in the choice of a way of life it makes; dialectic is tentative about those things that philosophy seeks to know, and sophistry is a seeming without a being.

{philospohy} {dialectic} {sophistry} {DISTINCTION} {RELATION} 
{bios}

{peirastikos} {gnōristikos}

<$chapter@2,deprivation,metaphysics,section@20,DISTINCTION,bekker@1004.1,Metaphysics/text,negation,PRIMARY@359.0,book@4,Metaphysics@23.0,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.15@ 
{negation} {deprivation} {DISTINCTION} 
ἔνθα μὲν οὖν †τῷ ἑνὶ ἡ διαφορὰ πρόσεστι παρὰ τὸ ἐν τῇ ἀποφάσει†, ἀπουσία γὰρ ἡ ἀπόφασις ἐκείνου ἐστίν, ἐν δὲ τῇ στερήσει καὶ ὑποκει- (15) μένη τις φύσις γίγνεται καθ’ ἧς λέγεται ἡ στέρησις) [τῷ δ’ ἑνὶ πλῆθος ἀντίκειται]-ὥστε καὶ τἀντικείμενα τοῖς εἰρη- μένοις, τό τε ἕτερον καὶ ἀνόμοιον καὶ ἄνισον καὶ ὅσα ἄλλα λέγεται ἢ κατὰ ταῦτα ἢ κατὰ πλῆθος καὶ τὸ ἕν, @1 τῆς εἰρημένης γνωρίζειν ἐπιστήμης· 
[Sachs] For we either say simply that that one thing is not present, or that it is not present in some class; in the latter case a difference is attached to the one thing aside from what is in the negation, since the negation is the absence of it, but in the deprivation some underlying nature comes along to which the deprivation is attributed.

<$chapter@2,PRIMARY@360.0,DEFINITION,Metaphysics@24.0,metaphysics,oppositeness,section@20,bekker@1004.1,Metaphysics/text,book@4,enantiotēs/gr,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.16@ {oppositeness} {DEFINITION} {enantiotēs/gr} 
_ὧν ἐστὶ καὶ ἡ ἐναντιότῆς εἰρημένης γνωρίζειν ἐπιστήμης· 
And among these is also oppositeness, since  oppositeness is a certain kind of ὧν ἐστὶ καὶ  ἡ ἐναντιό- (20) της· διαφορὰ γάρ τις ἡ ἐναντιότης,  ἡ δὲ διαφορὰ ἑτερό- της  

And among these is also oppositeness, since  oppositeness is a certain kind of difference,  and difference a kind of otherness. So since  oneness is meant in more than one way, these  two will be meant in more than one way, evern  though it belongs to one study to know them  all.

<$chapter@2,metaphysics,phainō/gr,314.19,bekker@1004.2,Metaphysics@26.0,oikeios,Metaphysics/text,tropos/gr,book@4,PRIMARY@362.0,truth,314.20,section@15,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.18@ 
{truth} 
καὶ ταῦτ’ ἐστὶ περὶ ὧν τοῦ φιλοσόφου ἐπισκέψασθαι @1 τὸ ἀληθές. σημεῖον δέ· οἱ γὰρ διαλεκτικοὶ καὶ σοφισταὶ τὸ αὐτὸ μὲν ὑποδύονται σχῆμα τῷ φιλοσόφῳ· 
And it belongs to the philosopher to investigate the truth concerning these things. 
But there is a sign. The dialecticians and the sophists put on the same form as the philosopher. [SACHS] Those who engage in dialectic and the sophists slip into the same outward appearance as the philosopher 

_σημεῖον δέ· οἱ γὰρ διαλεκτικοὶ καὶ σοφισταὶ τὸ αὐτὸ μὲν ὑποδύονται σχῆμα τῷ φιλοσόφῳ· 

ἡ γὰρ σο- φιστικὴ φαινομένη μόνον σοφία ἐστί, καὶ οἱ διαλεκτικοὶ διαλέγονται περὶ ἁπάντων, κοινὸν δὲ πᾶσι τὸ ὄν ἐστιν, (20) διαλέγονται δὲ περὶ τούτων δῆλον ὅτι διὰ τὸ τῆς φιλοσο- φίας ταῦτα εἶναι οἰκεῖα 
For sophistry is only apparent {phainō/gr} wisdom, and the dialecticians practice dialectic concerning all things, but the being is common to all things, and it is evident that they practice dialectic concerning all things since these things proper {oikeios} to philosophy.

περὶ μὲν γὰρ τὸ αὐτὸ γένος στρέ- φεται ἡ σοφιστικὴ καὶ ἡ διαλεκτικὴ τῇ φιλοσοφίᾳ, ἀλλὰ διαφέρει τῆς μὲν τῷ τρόπῳ τῆς δυνάμεως, τῆς δὲ τοῦ βίου τῇ προαιρέσει· 
For the sophistry and dialectics are turned toward the same class as philosophy, but the first differs from it in the direction {tropos/gr} of its power, and the second in the conduct of life.

<$chapter@2,metaphysics,CONCLUSION,bekker@1005.1,Metaphysics/text,book@4,Metaphysics@31.0,PRIMARY@367.0,section@14,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.23@ {CONCLUSION} of CHAPTER 2 
[Sachs] That then it belongs to one kind of knowlegde to study being as being and the things that belong to it insofar as it is being, is clear, and that the same contemplative study is about not only independent things but also what belongs to them, the ones mentioned as well as what is prior and posterior, genus and specieis, whole and part, and the other such things.

<$314.25,physicist,SUMMARY,truth,314.25.5.1.2,archē,nature,section@1,SCOPE,physics,314.25.5.2.1,logic,Moreoever@those who are engaged in specific forms of inquiry say nothing about them.,text@Metaphysics,bekker@1005.2,hypothesis,EXPLANATION,bebaiotatos,chapter@2,314.25.5.1,metaphysics,ultimate opinion,PRIMARY@368.0,INTRODUCTION,Metaphysics/text,philosophy,book@4,non-contradiction,Metaphysics@32.0 >
<@314.24@ CHAPTER 3 
{INTRODUCTION} 
[Sachs] One must discuss whether it belongs to one kind of knowledge or to different ones to be concerned with things that are called axioms in mathematics as well as with thinghood.

THE ANSWER SEEMS TO be that they both belong to {philosophy} {SCOPE}, insofar as philosophy is concerned with what belongs to all beings.

{SUMMARY} 
This is the case at least with the geometer or arithmetician; neither say anything about the "common" axioms. 
The {physicist} however does say something about them, since: 
[SACHS] The study of nature is a kind of wisdom too, but not the primary one. However much some of those who speak about truth try to say in what way one much receive it, they do this through a lack of education in the arts of logical analysis; for one must arrive already knowing about these things, and not find them out while studying.

{nature} {physics} 

{truth} 
[Sachs] So that the one who knows about being as being would be able to state the most certain sources of all things. And this is the philosopher. And the most certain of all principles is that about which it is impossible to be in error; for such a principle must be the best known (since all people are deceived about things they do not know) and nonhypothetical. For that which is necessary for one who understands any of the beings whatever to have is not a {hypothesis} {EXPLANATION}; and that which is necessary for him to arrive having.

THIS PRINCIPLE {archē} is the most certain {bebaiotatos} 

THIS PRINCIPLE = {non-contradiction} {logic} 
It is not possible for the same thing at the same time both to belong and not belong to the saem thing in the same respect...For it is impossible for anyone at all to conceive the same thing to be and not be, as some people thing Heracleitus says. 
For it is not necessary that someone take what he says into his understanding. 
AN {ultimate opinion}

<$chapter@2,metaphysics,doxa/gr,apodeixis,bekker@1005.2,section@30,Metaphysics@37.0,Metaphysics/text,book@4,PRIMARY@373.0,opinion,axiomata/gr,text@Metaphysics >

<$SUMMARY,bekker@1006.1,314.25.5.1.2,section@1,Metaphysics@39.0,book@1,314.25.5.2.1,text@Metaphysics,314.24,meaning,infinite regress,314.25.5.1,metaphysics,refutation,chapter@4,Metaphysics/text,Hence@"for while doing away with reason he submits to reason" [Sachs],non-contradiction,PRIMARY@375.0 >
<@314.25@ CHAPTER 4 {SUMMARY} 
Some do not accept the principle of non-contradiction, claiming that it is possible for the same thing to be and not be, and also claim that it is possible to conceive of something this way. 
We have argued that this is possible, and the {non-contradiction} is the most certain of all principles.

Yet want a demonstration of this. This is due to lack of education; it is lack of education not to know of what one ought to seek a demonstration and of what one ought not...

There cannot be a demonstration of everything {infinite regress} 
NEVERTHELESS; a kind of demonstration by {refutation} of {non-contradiction} is possible. 
WHAT follows is an intricate demonstration by refutation, which hinges upon the idea that whoever presents an argument against the principle of non-contradiction, and has actually said something rather than nothing, and *meant* {meaning} something in saying something, he will come into contradiction with himself, since he meant something determinate, yet what he is claiming denies the possibility of determinate meaning.

"Furthermore, the one who concedes this has conceded that something is true without demonstration"

^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-14 15:15:16.686034', '2021-03-16 09:22:49.045421', '2021-03-16 09:35:24.468346']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$section@25,metaphysics,orismenon,chapter@4,Metaphysics/text,Metaphysics@40.0,book@4,bekker@1006.1,PRIMARY@376.0,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.25.1@ . ἀρχὴ δὲ πρὸς ἅπαντα τὰ τοιαῦτα οὐ τὸ ἀξιοῦν ἢ εἶναί τι λέγειν ἢ μὴ εἶναι (τοῦτο μὲν γὰρ τάχ’ ἄν τις ὑπολάβοι τὸ ἐξ (20) ἀρχῆς αἰτεῖν), ἀλλὰ σημαίνειν γέ τι καὶ αὑτῷ καὶ ἄλλῳ· τοῦτο γὰρ ἀνάγκη, εἴπερ λέγοι τι. εἰ γὰρ μή, οὐκ ἂν εἴη τῷ τοιούτῳ λόγος, οὔτ’ αὐτῷ πρὸς αὑτὸν οὔτε πρὸς ἄλλον. ἂν δέ τις τοῦτο διδῷ, ἔσται ἀπόδειξις· ἤδη γάρ τι ἔσται ὡρισμένον. ἀλλ’ αἴτιος οὐχ ὁ ἀποδεικνὺς ἀλλ’ ὁ ὑπο- (25) μένων· ἀναιρῶν γὰρ λόγον ὑπομένει λόγον.

[Sachs] And the starting point for all such arguments is not the demand that one say something either to be or not to be (for perhaps one might suppose that this would require from the outset the thing to be shown), but that what he says must *mean* something to both himnself and someone else, for this is necessary, if he is going to say anything. For if this is not the case, there would be no argument with such a person, neither by himself in relation to himself, nor with anyone else. But if someone grants this, there will be a demonstration, for there will already by something determinate {orismenon}. But the one responsible for it is not the one demonstrating but the one who submits to it, for while doing away with reason he submits to reason. Furthermore, the one who concedes this has conceded that something is true without demonstration

<$DEFINITION,metaphysics,Metaphysics@42.0,chapter@4,PRIMARY@378.0,Metaphysics/text,book@4,bekker@1006.2,section@15,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.25.3@ 

_τὸ γὰρ μὴ ἓν σημαίνειν οὐθὲν σημαίνειν ἐστίν, μὴ σημαινόντων δὲ τῶν ὀνομάτων ἀνῄρηται τὸ διαλέγεσθαι πρὸς ἀλλήλους, κατὰ δὲ τὴν ἀλήθειαν καὶ πρὸς αὑτόν· οὐθὲν γὰρ ἐνδέχεται νοεῖν μὴ νοοῦντα ἕν, εἰ δ’ ἐνδέχεται, (10) τεθείη ἂν ὄνομα τούτῳ τῷ πράγματι ἕν). -ἔστω δή, ὥσπερ ἐλέχθη κατ’ ἀρχάς, σημαῖνόν τι τὸ ὄνομα καὶ σημαῖνον ἕν· οὐ δὴ ἐνδέχεται τὸ ἀνθρώπῳ εἶναι σημαίνειν ὅπερ ἀνθρώπῳ μὴ εἶναι, εἰ τὸ ἄνθρωπος σημαίνει μὴ μόνον καθ’ ἑνὸς ἀλλὰ καὶ ἕν (οὐ γὰρ τοῦτο ἀξιοῦμεν τὸ ἓν σημαίνειν, (15) τὸ καθ’ ἑνός, ἐπεὶ οὕτω γε κἂν τὸ μουσικὸν καὶ τὸ λευκὸν καὶ τὸ ἄνθρωπος ἓν ἐσήμαινεν, ὥστε ἓν ἅπαντα ἔσται· καὶ τὸ ἄνθρωπος ἓν ἐσήμαινεν, ὥστε ἓν ἅπαντα ἔσται· συνώνυμα γάρ). καὶ οὐκ ἔσται εἶναι καὶ μὴ εἶναι τὸ αὐτὸ ἀλλ’ ἢ καθ’ ὁμωνυμίαν, ὥσπερ ἂν εἰ ὃν ἡμεῖς ἄνθρωπον καλοῦμεν, ἄλλοι μὴ ἄνθρωπον καλοῖεν· 
(also a bit earlier) 
THE ARGUMENT: 
1) If someone says anything at all they must *mean* something to themselves and to someone else.

2) If someone says nothing, there is no argument with them; they are like a plant 

3) IF (1), then they have said something definite, words "be" and "no be" have a definite meaning. 
(4) Further, suppose that, if "human being" signifies something definite, it is "two-footed definition" 

(5) {DEFINITION} signifying one thing = "If this is a human being, then if anything is a human being, this will be its human-being "

"For it is not possible to think without thinking one thing, so if thinking is possible, one could set down one name for this thing." 
i.e. : if thinking is possible at all, then words have defininite meaning...

<$314.25,bekker@1007.1,truth,314.25.5.1.2,principle,PRIMARY@382.0,Metaphysics@46.0,project@TRUTHBOOK1,314.25.5.2.1,logic,REFLECTIONS@36.0,REFLECTION,text@Metaphysics,314.24,section@20,ontology,Plato,Parmenides,metaphysics,chapter@4,being,Metaphysics/text,book@4,non-contradiction >
<@314.25.5.1@ {REFLECTION} 
THIS gets at the most fundamental point...The intertwinement of {truth} and {being}...The definiteness of meaning depends on the {logic}al {principle} of {non-contradiction}...

But this in turn is bound up with {ontology}; to deny {non-contradiction} not only destroys the possibility of truth, but of being...

The one destruction and the other are the same...

// 
IN a way, this returns Aristotle to the Parmendian premise, yet, unlike {Plato}, and unlike {Parmenides}, he does not conceive of this foundational logic as some sort of {truth} to which we need special access, or a kind of demonstration...The basic possibility of truth is bound up with, and structures, the realm of ordinary opinion.

<$314.25,Metaphysics@109.0,epistēm/emacron,principle of non-contradiction,Tractatus,PNC,Wittgenstein,SECONDARY@3.0,314.24,type@translation,author@Christopher Kirwin,correspondence truth,PRIMARY@445.0,314.25.5.1,compare@Posterior Analytics,metaphysics,Metaphysics/text,pubdate@1991,deflated,page@204 >
<@314.25.5.1.2@ The Posterior Analytics tells us that only what can be demonstrated is episteton, i.e. can be understood, i.e. is part of a "discipline" or science (71b1619 with 100b1011), and this raises the question: if PNC cannot be demonstrated, what are these chapters about it doing in a treatise on the "discipline" of metaphysics? There arc three main possibilities. (i) Perhaps by the time he wrote Metaphysics F Aristotle had abandoned his earlier view that only what can be demonstrated is episteton, and had come to believe that 'objectivity' is also attainable by a kind of dialectic.
This is the thesis of [37] and the unifying theme of [38]. (ii) Aristotle had not abandoned his view that sciences must proceed by demonstration; so since the principles of noncontradiction and excluded middle are indemonstrable, Aristotle's treatment of them in G 38 is not an exercise in the science of metaphysics as the Note on 1005b8 asserts, but a prolegomenon or a digression. (iii) G 38 aim at demonstration, but what they aim to demonstrate is theses about the principles of noncontradiction and excluded middle, not those indemonstrable principles themselves; see [39]. It will be clear that the preceding paragraph adopts (iii) as solving the problem; but two further comments are in order.
First, this solution would not prevent (i) and the conclusion of (ii) from being true also. Secondly, it is important to notice that G 38 are exceptional within tile Metaphysics-indeed within the Aristotclian corpus-in aiming at demonstration, or at least sketching a method of demonstration. Aristotle's usual procedure, which is not to offer demonstrations but rather to invite his audience to join him in inquiry, is quite compatible with his believing that the results of successful philosophical inquiry can be set out in demonstrations, so qualifying as sciences even according to the canons of the Posterior Analytics.

{compare@Posterior Analytics} {epistēm/emacron} 

// IT SEEMS to me though that this comment does not really get at the heart of what is going on here...Aristotle is addressing his argument at the sophist, the one who argues for the sake of argument...

THIS is very important, since it suggests not merely that it is an kind of digression, or appendix, but that it is presented with a view to arming the metaphysician against a certain kind of specious argument that has been made.

BUT at the same time: the PNC clearly does have a special status; it is akin to what {Wittgenstein} in the {Tractatus} speaks of as a logical space...In some ways, though Aristotle is more radical and consequent that Wittgenstein, since he does not attribute features to this logical space that are more restrictive than need be, such as a "picture" theory of truth...
We could say--for Aristotle the logical space of language the {principle of non-contradiction} {PNC} -- as indeed the most {deflated} version of {correspondence truth}

<$314.25,314.25.5.1,314.24 >
<@314.25.5.2.1@ {COMMENTARY} {Christopher Kirwin} {SECONDARY} {author@Christopher Kirwan} 
{title@Aristotle Metaphysics Books Gamma, Delta, Epsilon} 
{non-contradiction} {PNC} 

_The Posterior Analytics tells us that only what can be demonstrated is episteton, i.e. can be understood, i.e. is part of a "discipline" or science (71b1619 with 100b1011), and this raises the question: if PNC cannot Pbe demonstrated, what are these chapters about it doing in a treatise on the "discipline" of metaphysics? There arc three main possibilities. (i) Perhaps by the time he wrote Metaphysics F Aristotle had abandoned his earlier view that only what can be demonstrated is episteton, and had come to believe that 'objectivity' is also attainable by a kind of dialectic.
This is the thesis of [37] and the unifying theme of [38]. (ii) Aristotle had not abandoned his view that sciences must proceed by demonstration; so since the principles of noncontradiction and excluded middle are indemonstrable, Aristotle's treatment of them in G 38 is not an exercise 
_in the science of metaphysics as the Note on 1005b8 asserts, but a prolegomenon or a digression. (iii) G 38 aim at demonstration, but what they aim to demonstrate is theses about the principles of noncontradiction and excluded middle, not those indemonstrable principles themselves; see [39]. It will be clear that the preceding paragraph adopts (iii) as solving the problem; but two further comments are in order.
First, this solution would not prevent (i) and the conclusion of (ii) from being true also. Secondly, it is important to notice that G 38 are exceptional within tile Metaphysics-indeed within the Aristotclian 
_corpus-in aiming at demonstration, or at least sketching a method of demonstration. Aristotle's usual procedure, which is not to offer demonstrations but rather to invite his audience to join him in inquiry, is quite compatible with his believing that the results of successful philosophical 
_inquiry can be set out in demonstrations, so qualifying as sciences even 
_according to the canons of the Posterior Analytics.

_{compare@Posterior Analytics} 
{epistēm/emacron} 

__/// 
_IT SEEMS to me though that this comment does not really get at the heart of what is going on here...Aristotle is addressing his argument at the sophist, the one who argues for the sake of argument...

__THIS is very important, since it suggests not merely that it is an kind of digression, or appendix, but that it is presented with a view to arming the metaphysician against a certain kind of specious argument that has been made.

__BUT at the same time: the PNC clearly does have a special status; it is akin to what {Wittgenstein} in the {Tractatus} speaks of as a logical space...In some ways, though Aristotle is more radical and consequent that Wittgenstein, since he does not attribute features to this logical space that are more restrictive than need be, such as a "picture" theory of truth...

__We could say--for Aristotle the logical space of language the {principle of non-contradiction} {PNC} -- as indeed the most {deflated} version of {correspondence truth}

<$metaphysics,section@20,PRIMARY@383.0,bekker@1007.1,chapter@4,Metaphysics/text,book@4,Metaphysics@47.0,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.25.6@ τὸ δ’ οὐσίαν σημαίνειν ἐστὶν ὅτι οὐκ ἄλλο τι τὸ εἶναι αὐτῷ. εἰ δ’ ἔσται αὐτῷ τὸ ὅπερ ἀνθρώπῳ εἶναι ἢ ὅπερ μὴ ἀνθρώπῳ εἶναι ἢ ὅπερ μὴ εἶναι ἀνθρώπῳ, ἄλλο ἔσται, ὥστ’ ἀναγκαῖον αὐτοῖς λέγειν ὅτι οὐθενὸς ἔσται τοιοῦτος λόγος, ἀλλὰ πάντα 
λέγειν ὅτι οὐθενὸς ἔσται τοιοῦτος λόγος, ἀλλὰ πάντα (30) κατὰ συμβεβηκός 

[Sachs] But to signify thinghood is to mean that nothing else is the being of it. But if the very thing that it is to be human is the same as the very thing that it is either not to be human or to be not human, then something else *would* be the being of it, so that they would have to say that nothing has any such defining articulation, but all things are incidental; for in this respect thinghood and what is incidental are distinguished.

_εἰ δὲ μὴ ἐπιστάμενοι ἀλλὰ δοξάζοντες, πολὺ μᾶλλον ἐπιμελητέον ἂν εἴη τῆς ἀληθείας, ὥσπερ καὶ νοσώδει ὄντι ἢ ὑγιεινῷ τῆς ὑγιείας· καὶ γὰρ ὁ δοξάζων πρὸς τὸν ἐπιστάμενον οὐχ ὑγιεινῶς διά- (30) κειται πρὸς τὴν ἀλήθειαν.

For one who has an opinion, as compared with the one who has knowledge, is not disposed in a healthy way toward {truth}

<@314.25.12@ εἰ οὖν τὸ (1009a) μᾶλλον ἐγγύτερον, εἴη γε ἄν τι ἀληθὲς οὗ ἐγγύτερον τὸ μᾶλλον ἀληθές. κἂν εἰ μὴ ἔστιν, ἀλλ’ ἤδη γέ τι ἔστι βε- βαιότερον καὶ ἀληθινώτερον, καὶ τοῦ λόγου ἀπηλλαγμέ- νοι ἂν εἴημεν τοῦ ἀκράτου καὶ κωλύοντός τι τῇ διανοίᾳ ὁρίσαι. (5)

<$compare@Husserl,Heracliteans/school,motion,taste,SUMMARY,compare@Locke,truth,perception,Empedocles,Cratylus/philosopher,appearance,relativism/position,DIFFERENCE,primary ousia,idealism,text@Metaphysics,potentiality,universe,chapter@5,Democritus,becoming,Protagoras,Parmenides,Anaxagoras,metaphysics,Metaphysics/text,Metaphysics@57.0,book@4,PRIMARY@393.0,compare@Brentano,intentionality,actuality,bekker@1009.1,authoritative >
<@314.26@ CHAPTER 5 {SUMMARY} 
THE prounouncement of {Protagoras} ["A human being is the measure of all things---of the things that are, that they are, and of the things that are not, that they are not"] 
is a consequence of the opinion treated in the previous section, "for if all opinions and appearances are true, it is necessary that all things be at the same time both true and false" = {relativism/position} 
And yet the approach Aristotle will take to refute this opinion is very different. "for some people need persuasion while others need brute force" 
Those who believe this because they are at an impasse are easy to cure; but for whose who are for the sake of arguing, the remedy is a reducation of their exact vocal and verbal argument.

THOSE who are at an impasse take their departure from perception, since contradictories and contraries are present at the same time.

IN RESPONSE to these: they are in some way right, in some way wrong. Being is said in two ways; "so that there is a way in which it is possible for something to come into being out of what is not, and a way in whcih it is not,..." 
{potentiality} {actuality} {DIFFERENCE} 
{Democritus} {Empedocles} {Parmenides} {Anaxagoras} 
It is very troubling then that even these philosophers have come to this opinion; is the pursuit of {truth} futile.

YET THESE opinions can be explained from the fact that they assumed that the only beings were perceptible things. 
And moreover they saw everything as being in {motion} 
Leading to the opinions of the {Heracliteans/school} such as {Cratylus/philosopher} 
To resolve these difficulties: "to change in quantity is not the same as to change what sort of thing something is, so letting something not stay constant with respect to size, we will recognize everything on account of its form" 
Those with this opinion about {becoming} nevertheless deserve to be censored, since they extend this opinion to the whole {universe} 
Draws a distinction between {appearance} and {perception} 
Also introduces the concept of the {authoritative} -- The opinion of a doctor regarding the future, or of one sense over another. 
"But not even at a different time does one of the senses disagree about the *attribute*, but only about that to which the attribute belongs.

"

This even applies to taste..."but the sweetness itself, such as it *is* whenever it might be present, never changes" {compare@Locke} 
REJECTION of {idealism} vis a vis {primary ousia} 
"And in general, if all there is is what is perceptible, nothing would be if there were not beings with souls, since there would be no sense perceptions. Now that there would be neither sense objects nor sense perceptions is perhaps true (for these are experiences of the perceiver), but it is impossible that there not be the underlying things which bring about sense perception, even without the perception. For the perception itself is sure not *of* itself, but there is also something else besides the perception, which must be prior to the perception" 
{compare@Brentano} {compare@Husserl} {intentionality}

<$aporia,metaphysics,chapter@5,false,Metaphysics@58.0,section@10,Metaphysics/text,book@4,PRIMARY@394.0,true,sophism,Protagoras,bekker@1009.1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.26.1@ Ἔστι δ’ ἀπὸ τῆς αὐτῆς δόξης καὶ ὁ Πρωταγόρου λόγος, καὶ ἀνάγκη ὁμοίως αὐτοὺς ἄμφω ἢ εἶναι ἢ μὴ εἶναι· εἴτε γὰρ τὰ δοκοῦντα πάντα ἐστὶν ἀληθῆ καὶ τὰ φαινόμενα, γὰρ τὰ δοκοῦντα πάντα ἐστὶν ἀληθῆ καὶ τὰ φαινόμενα, ἀνάγκη εἶναι πάντα ἅμα ἀληθῆ καὶ ψευδῆ (πολλοὶ γὰρ τἀναντία ὑπολαμβάνουσιν ἀλλήλοις, καὶ τοὺς μὴ ταὐτὰ (10) δοξάζοντας ἑαυτοῖς διεψεῦσθαι νομίζουσιν· 
As a consequence of the same opinion, there is also the pronouncement of {Protagoras}, and they must both alike either be so or not be so; for if all opinions and appearances are true, it is necessary that all things be at the same time both {true} and {false} (for many people conceive of things that are contrary to one another, and consider those who do not believe the same things as themselves to be wrong, so that the same thing must both be and not), and if this is so, all opinions must be true (for people who are wrong and those who are right believe things opposite to one another, so if things are this way, everything is right).

ὥστ’ ἀνάγκη τὸ αὐτὸ εἶναί τε καὶ μὴ εἶναι), καὶ εἰ τοῦτ’ ἔστιν, ἀνάγκη τὰ δοκοῦντα εἶναι πάντ’ ἀληθῆ (τὰ ἀντικείμενα γὰρ δοξάζουσιν ἀλλήλοις οἱ διεψευσμένοι καὶ ἀληθεύοντες· εἰ οὖν ἔχει τὰ ὄντα οὕτως, ἀληθεύσουσι πάντες). ὅτι μὲν οὖν ἀπὸ τῆς αὐτῆς (15) εἰσὶ διανοίας ἀμφότεροι οἱ λόγοι, δῆλον· ἔστι δ’ οὐχ ὁ αὐτὸς τρόπος πρὸς ἅπαντας τῆς ἐντεύξεως· οἱ μὲν γὰρ πει- θοῦς δέονται οἱ δὲ βίας. ὅσοι μὲν γὰρ ἐκ τοῦ ἀπορῆσαι ὑπέλαβον οὕτως, τούτων εὐΐατος ἡ ἄγνοια (οὐ γὰρ πρὸς τὸν λόγον ἀλλὰ πρὸς τὴν διάνοιαν ἡ ἀπάντησις αὐτῶν)· ὅσοι (20) δὲ λόγου χάριν λέγουσι, τούτων δ’ ἔλεγχος ἴασις τοῦ ἐν τῇ φωνῇ λόγου καὶ τοῦ ἐν τοῖς ὀνόμασιν 
So it is clear that both of these arguments are consequences of the same thinking. But the same way of going about things is not possible in every encounter; for some people need persuasion while others need brute force. For all those who believe this way from being at an impasse in thought, their ignorance is easy to cure (for the engagement is not with their argument but with their thinking), but for those who argue for the sake of arguing, the remedy for them is a refutation of their exact vocal and verbal argument.

{sophism} {aporia}

<$aporia,bias/gr,peithos/gr,REFLECTIONS@42.0,Metaphysics@59.0,metaphysics,PRIMARY@395.0,chapter@5,REFLECTION,project@TRUTHBOOK1,DIFFERENCE,section@10,Metaphysics/text,book@4,perception,bekker@1009.1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.26.1.1@ SO the same problematic has two dimensions: one which is attributed to a kind of sophistic argument, and one which results from an {aporia} {REFLECTION} 
The manner of confronting these is completely different: IN the first case, the argument itself will be refuted, but showing that the person making the argument has already committed himself to the position that he opposes.

In the second case, the mistaken thinking that underlies the argument will be confronted...

The mistaken thinking underlying the argument results from failing to distinguish between {perception} and that which underlies perception. Failing to recongize that being is meant in two ways ---potentiality and actuality.

{bias/gr} {peithos/gr} {DIFFERENCE}

<$metaphysics,bekker@1009.2,chapter@5,Metaphysics@61.0,section@30,Metaphysics/text,book@4,PRIMARY@397.0,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.26.3@ 
_φασὶ δὲ καὶ τὸν Ὅμηρον ταύτην ἔχοντα φαίνε- σθαι τὴν δόξαν, ὅτι ἐποίησε τὸν Ἕκτορα, ὡς ἐξέστη ὑπὸ τῆς πληγῆς, κεῖσθαι ἀλλοφρονέοντα, ὡς φρονοῦντας μὲν (30) καὶ τοὺς παραφρονοῦντας ἀλλ’ οὐ ταὐτά. δῆλον οὖν ὅτι, εἰ ἀμφότεραι φρονήσεις, καὶ τὰ ὄντα ἅμα οὕτω τε καὶ οὐχ οὕτως ἔχει. ᾗ καὶ χαλεπώτατον τὸ συμβαῖνόν ἐστιν· εἰ γὰρ οἱ μάλιστα τὸ ἐνδεχόμενον ἀληθὲς ἑωρακότες-οὗτοι δ’ εἰσὶν οἱ μάλιστα ζητοῦντες αὐτὸ καὶ φιλοῦντες-οὗτοι τοι- (35) αύτας ἔχουσι τὰς δόξας καὶ ταῦτα ἀποφαίνονται περὶ τῆς ἀληθείας, πῶς οὐκ ἄξιον ἀθυμῆσαι τοὺς φιλοσοφεῖν ἐγχειροῦντας; τὸ γὰρ τὰ πετόμενα διώκειν τὸ ζητεῖν ἂν (1010a) εἴη τὴν ἀλήθειαν. 

And people also say that Homer seemed to have this opinion, because he made Hector, when he was knocked out by a blow, lie "thinking changed thoughts," as though even those who are delirious are thinking, just not the same things. So it is clear that, if both are processes of thinking, the beings too are at the same time so and not so. And it is in this respect that what follows is most harsh: for if those who most of all have seen the truth that is accessible--and these are those who seek it most and love it most---if they have such opinions and declare these things about truth, how will this not be enough to make those who are trying to philosophize lose heart? For seeking the truth would be a wild goose chase

<$metaphysics,Metaphysics@62.0,chapter@5,Metaphysics/text,PRIMARY@398.0,book@4,bekker@1010.1,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.26.4@ αἴτιον δὲ τῆς δόξης τούτοις ὅτι περὶ τῶν ὄντων μὲν τὴν ἀλήθειαν ἐσκόπουν, τὰ δ’ ὄντα ὑπέλαβον εἶναι τὰ αἰσθητὰ μόνον· ἐν δὲ τούτοις πολλὴ ἡ τοῦ ἀορίστου φύσις ἐνυπάρχει καὶ ἡ τοῦ ὄντος οὕτως ὥσπερ εἴπομεν· 
[Sachs, as before] 
Now the cause of their opinion is that they were inquiring into the truth about beings, but they assumed that the only beings were perceptible things; but amogn these the nature of the indeterminate is heavily present, and the sort of being that we described. 
For this reason, while they speak reasonably, they do not speak the truth. 
διὸ εἰκότως μὲν λέγουσιν, οὐκ ἀληθῆ δὲ λέγουσιν (οὕτω γὰρ (5) ἁρμόττει μᾶλλον εἰπεῖν ἢ ὥσπερ Ἐπίχαρμος εἰς Ξενοφά- νην).

<@314.26.5@ 
_ἔτι δὲ πᾶσαν ὁρῶντες ταύτην κινουμένην τὴν φύσιν, κατὰ δὲ τοῦ μεταβάλλοντος οὐθὲν ἀληθευόμενον, περί γε τὸ πάντῃ πάντως μεταβάλλον οὐκ ἐνδέχεσθαι ἀληθεύειν.

<$metaphysics,chapter@5,phantasia/gr,aisthesis/gr,PRIMARY@402.0,DIFFERENCE,bekker@1010.2,Metaphysics/text,RELATION,-448,book@4,Metaphysics@66.0,section@5,314.26.6.1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.26.6@ περὶ δὲ τῆς ἀληθείας, ὡς οὐ πᾶν τὸ φαινόμενον ἀληθές, πρῶτον μὲν ὅτι οὐδ’  ἡ αἴσθησις  ψευδὴς τοῦ γε ἰδίου ἐστίν, ἀλλ’ ἡ φαντασία οὐ ταὐτὸν τῇ αἰσθήσει. εἶτ’ ἄξιον θαυμάσαι εἰ τοῦτ’ ἀποροῦσι, πότερον τηλικαῦτά ἐστι τὰ μεγέθη καὶ τὰ χρώματα τοιαῦτα οἷα τοῖς ἄπωθεν φαίνεται ἢ οἷα τοῖς ἐγγύθεν, καὶ πότερον οἷα τοῖς ὑγιαίνουσιν ἢ οἷα τοῖς κάμνουσιν, καὶ βαρύτερα πότερον ἃ τοῖς ἀσθε- νοῦσιν ἢ ἃ τοῖς ἰσχύουσιν, καὶ ἀληθῆ πότερον ἃ τοῖς κα- θεύδουσιν ἢ ἃ τοῖς ἐγρηγορόσιν. ὅτι μὲν γὰρ οὐκ οἴονταί γε, φανερόν 

But as far as truth is concerned, that not every appearance is true is because, in the first place, even if the perception, at least of the proper object of each of the senses is not false, still appearance is not the same thing as perception.

{phantasia/gr} VS {aisthesis/gr} {DIFFERENCE}

<$REFLECTIONS@47.0,relativism,metaphysics,chapter@6,most established opinion/syntagm,Metaphysics/text,Metaphysics@71.0,bekker@1011.1,SUMMARY,book@4,bebaiotatē doxa/syntagm,PRIMARY@407.0,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.27@ CHAPTER 6 {SUMMARY} 
THIS brings to a conclusion the critique of {relativism} etc presented in the last two chapters...

"So to the effect that the best established of all opinions is that contradictory statements are not true at the same time, and what follows for those who say that they are, and they talk that way, let so much have been said. But since it is impossible for a contradiction to be *true*& at one time or the same thing, it is clear that neither could contrary properties *belong* to the same thing at the same time...So if it is impossible at the same time to assert and deny truly, it is also impossible for contraries to be present together, unless both are present in certain respects, or one of them in a certain respect, and the other simply." 

_Ὅτι μὲν οὖν βεβαιοτάτη δόξα πασῶν τὸ μὴ εἶναι ἀληθεῖς ἅμα τὰς ἀντικειμένας φάσεις, καὶ τί συμβαίνει τοῖς οὕτω λέγουσι, καὶ διὰ τί οὕτω λέγουσι, τοσαῦτα εἰρήσθω· ἐπεὶ (15) δ’ ἀδύνατον τὴν ἀντίφασιν ἅμα ἀληθεύεσθαι κατὰ τοῦ αὐτοῦ, φανερὸν ὅτι οὐδὲ τἀναντία ἅμα ὑπάρχειν ἐνδέχεται τῷ αὐτῷ· τῶν μὲν γὰρ ἐναντίων θάτερον στέρησίς ἐστιν οὐχ ἧττον, οὐσίας δὲ στέρησις· ἡ δὲ στέρησις ἀπόφασίς ἐστιν ἀπό τινος ὡρισμένου γένους· εἰ οὖν ἀδύνατον ἅμα καταφάναι καὶ @1 (20) ἀποφάναι ἀληθῶς, ἀδύνατον καὶ τἀναντία ὑπάρχειν ἅμα, ἀλλ’ ἢ πῇ ἄμφω ἢ θάτερον μὲν πῇ θάτερον δὲ ἁπλῶς.

<$truth,true,political,modes,REFLECTIONS@49.0,ordering,compare@Heidegger,project@TRUTHBOOK1,Metaphysics@75.0,text@Metaphysics,REFLECTION,section@20,DISTINCTION,Being and Time/book,metaphysics,chapter@6,strata,speculative,Metaphysics/text,bekker@1011.1,PRIMARY@411.0,book@4,mode >
<@314.27.2.1@ {REFLECTION} 
With this passage, Aristotle has managed to reconcile what seems to be conflicting positions about whether perception is simply true, or neither true or false...

Appearance is simply {true} to the one for whom it appears, as it appears...THIS is the phenomenological level of disclosure...
 Needless to say {compare@Heidegger} this is key to Heidegger's account in {Being and Time/book} 
// {strata} 
These strata are not the same as MODES of truth...Rather: we could say that FOR Aristotle these strata of truth are a way of organizing the {modes} of truth...A kind of {ordering} of the modes of {truth}...

BUT ultimately...AND this is the point of my book: the politico-speculative ordering, achieved via neoplatonism and Christianity, will win out over Aristotle's approach...
Could we say that that Aristotle distinguishes between the {political} and the {speculative} {ordering}, rather than conflating them...

<@314.27.3@ But if it is not the case that everything is relative, but some things are themselves in their own right, then not everything that appears would be true. For what appears is an appearance to something, so that the one who says that all appearances are true makes all beings relative. And for this reason, those who are looking for the brute force of argument, and at the same time insist on putting forth a reason, ought to beware, since it is not the appearance that is true, but the appearance to the one it appears to, and when it appears and in what respect and how.

<$320,PRIMARY@413.0,DEFINITION,metaphysics,FORMULATION,section@20,314.28,Metaphysics/text,book@4,truth,law,bekker@1011.2,Metaphysics@77.0,chapter@7,excluded middle,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.28.1@ {FORMULATION} {law} of {excluded middle} 
Ἀλλὰ μὴν οὐδὲ μεταξὺ ἀντιφάσεως ἐνδέχεται εἶναι οὐθέν, ἀλλ’ ἀνάγκη ἢ φάναι ἢ ἀποφάναι ἓν καθ’ ἑνὸς ὁτιοῦν.
δῆλον δὲ πρῶτον μὲν ὁρισαμένοις τί τὸ ἀληθὲς καὶ ψεῦδος. (25) τὸ μὲν γὰρ λέγειν τὸ ὂν μὴ εἶναι ἢ τὸ μὴ ὂν εἶναι ψεῦ- δος, τὸ δὲ τὸ ὂν εἶναι καὶ τὸ μὴ ὂν μὴ εἶναι ἀληθές, ὥστε καὶ ὁ λέγων εἶναι ἢ μὴ ἀληθεύσει ἢ ψεύσεται· ἀλλ’ οὔτε τὸ ὂν λέγεται μὴ εἶναι ἢ εἶναι οὔτε τὸ μὴ ὄν.

[Sachs] But neither is it possible that there be anything between contradictories, but about any one thing whatever it is necessary either to affirm or deny one of them. 
This is evident first of all to those who define what the true or false are. For to say that what is is not or that what is not is, is false, but to say that what is is and what is not is not, is true, so that the one who says that something is or is not is either right or wrong. But there is no middle ground, niether what is nor what is not is said either to be or not to be. 
{DEFINITION} of {truth}

<$DEFINITION,metaphysics,definition,section@20,PRIMARY@417.0,bekker@1012.1,Metaphysics/text,Heraclitus,Metaphysics@81.0,onoma,book@4,signification,sēeinon/gr,name,logos,chapter@7,Anaxagoras,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.28.3@ {DEFINITION} of {definition} 

_οἱ μὲν οὖν διὰ τοιαύτην αἰτίαν (20) λέγουσιν, οἱ δὲ διὰ τὸ πάντων ζητεῖν λόγον. ἀρχὴ δὲ πρὸς ἅπαντας τούτους ἐξ ὁρισμοῦ. ὁρισμὸς δὲ γίγνεται ἐκ τοῦ ση- μαίνειν τι ἀναγκαῖον εἶναι αὐτούς· ὁ γὰρ λόγος οὗ τὸ ὄνομα σημεῖον ὁρισμὸς ἔσται. ἔοικε δ’ ὁ μὲν Ἡρακλείτου λόγος, λέγων πάντα εἶναι καὶ μὴ εἶναι, ἅπαντα ἀληθῆ (25) ποιεῖν, ὁ δ’ Ἀναξαγόρου, εἶναί τι μεταξὺ τῆς ἀντιφάσεως πάντα ψευδῆ· ὅταν γὰρ μιχθῇ, οὔτε ἀγαθὸν οὔτε οὐκ ἀγαθὸν τὸ μῖγμα, ὥστ’ οὐδὲν εἰπεῖν ἀληθές.

[Sachs] But the starting point for all of these comes from definition. And the definition arises from the necessity that they mean something, since the articulation of which the word is a sign will be a definition. But while the Heraclitean account {Heraclitus}, saying that everything is and is not, seemed to make all things be true, that of {Anaxagoras}, that there is something between contradictories, seems to make all things false, for when things are mixed, the mixture is neither good nor not-good, so that there is nothing true to say.

// THE definition is {logos} ; the {name} {onoma} is a sign {sēeinon/gr} of the logos.

SO the name signifies {signification} the definition...

<$bekker@1012.2,metaphysics,section@20,chapter@8,Metaphysics@82.0,PRIMARY@418.0,prime mover,physics,Metaphysics/text,Heraclitus,infinite regress/proof,SUMMARY,book@4,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.29@ CHAPTER 8 {SUMMARY} 
THIS final chapter of book gamma of the Metaphysics seems to offer a summary of the entire argument up to this point.

The fundamental conclusion: one-sided statements with general application--all things are true, all things are false--cannot be accepted in the way people say them. 

HENCE: one also cannot say that {Heraclitus} that " all things are true " and "all things are false" 
Since if each is impossible, so is the conjunction.

CONFRONTED with such claims one must simply insist simply that words means something. "so that one must reason from a definition to grasp what would mean something false or something true" 
"And if what is true to assert is what is false to deny, it is impossible for all things to be false, since one of the two parts of the contradiction must be true" = SO in other words: if one means that something is false, one also mneans that something is true.

Moreover, if is is necessary to either (1) assert everything or (2) deny everything, then either (1) or (2) false; both cannot be false.

"For the one who says that everything is true also makes the statement contrary to his own to be true, so that his own is not true" 
NOR can one make just one exception, because if this exception is true, the statement that it is true is also true, ad infinitum {infinite regress/proof} 
AND FURTHERMORE, this leads to a conclusion about {physics}..."For if all things are at rest, the same things would always be true and the same things false, but this obviously changes, but if all things are in motion, nothing would be true and therefore everything would be false, but it has been demonstrated that this is impossible." 
AND FINALLY the cosmological conclusion: "But it is surely not everything that is at rest sometimes and in motion sometimes, while nothing is always at rest or in motion, for there is something that is always moving the things that move, and the first mover itself is motionless." {prime mover} 


^:user|S|USER:^
^:date|L|['2021-03-15 10:43:55.309761']:^
^:size|I|60:^ >

<$bekker@1012.2,metaphysics,chapter@8,PRIMARY@420.0,Metaphysics@84.0,section@10,Metaphysics/text,book@4,text@Metaphysics >
<@314.29.2@ ἀλλὰ πρὸς πάντας τοὺς τοιούτους λόγους αἰτεῖσθαι δεῖ, κα- (5) θάπερ ἐλέχθη καὶ ἐν τοῖς ἐπάνω λόγοις, οὐχὶ εἶναί τι ἢ μὴ εἶναι ἀλλὰ σημαίνειν τι, ὥστε ἐξ ὁρισμοῦ διαλεκτέον λα- @1 βόντας τί σημαίνει τὸ ψεῦδος ἢ τὸ ἀληθές. εἰ δὲ μηθὲν ἄλλο τὸ ἀληθὲς φάναι ἢ  ἀποφάναι ψεῦδός ἐστιν, ἀδύ- νατον πάντα ψευδῆ εἶναι· ἀνάγκη γὰρ τῆς ἀντιφάσεως (10) θάτερον εἶναι μόριον ἀληθές 
[Sachs] But in the face of all such claims one must require what was said in the chapters above, not that something be or not be so but that it *mean* something, so that one must reason from a definition to grasp what would mean something false or something true. And if what is true to assert is what is false to deny, it is impossible for all things to be false, since one of the two parts of the contradiction must be true. Further, if it is necessary either to assert or deny everything, it is impossible for all things to be false, since one of the two parts of the contradiction must be true.

<$metaphysics,PRIMARY@422.0,section@20,false,Metaphysics@86.0,Metaphysics/text,bekker@1024.2,chapter@29,book@5,text@Metaphysics >
<@319@ THE DISCUSSION OF THE MULTIPLE MEANINGS OF {false} in the fifth book of the Metaphysics, Delta 
Τὸ ψεῦδος λέγεται ἄλλον μὲν τρόπον ὡς πρᾶγμα ψεῦδος, καὶ τούτου τὸ μὲν τῷ μὴ συγκεῖσθαι ἢ ἀδύνατον εἶναι συντεθῆναι (ὥσπερ λέγεται τὸ τὴν διάμετρον εἶναι σύμμετρον ἢ τὸ σὲ καθῆσθαι· τούτων γὰρ ψεῦδος τὸ μὲν (20) ἀεὶ τὸ δὲ ποτέ· οὕτω γὰρ οὐκ ὄντα ταῦτα), τὰ δὲ ὅσα ἔστι μὲν ὄντα, πέφυκε μέντοι φαίνεσθαι ἢ μὴ οἷά ἐστιν ἢ ἃ μὴ ἔστιν (οἷον ἡ σκιαγραφία καὶ τὰ ἐνύπνια· ταῦτα γὰρ ἔστι μέν τι, ἀλλ’ οὐχ ὧν ἐμποιεῖ τὴν φαντασίαν)· -πράγματα μὲν οὖν ψευδῆ οὕτω λέγεται, ἢ τῷ μὴ εἶναι αὐτὰ ἢ τῷ (25) τὴν ἀπ’ αὐτῶν φαντασίαν μὴ ὄντος εἶναι· λόγος δὲ ψευ- @1 δὴς ὁ τῶν μὴ ὄντων, ᾗ ψευδής, διὸ πᾶς λόγος ψευδὴς ἑτέ- ρου ἢ οὗ ἐστὶν ἀληθής, οἷον ὁ τοῦ κύκλου ψευδὴς τριγώνου.
ἑκάστου δὲ λόγος ἔστι μὲν ὡς εἷς, ὁ τοῦ τί ἦν εἶναι, ἔστι δ’ ὡς πολλοί, ἐπεὶ ταὐτό πως αὐτὸ καὶ αὐτὸ πεπονθός, οἷον Σω- (30) κράτης καὶ Σωκράτης μουσικός (ὁ δὲ ψευδὴς λόγος οὐθενός ἐστιν ἁπλῶς λόγος)· διὸ Ἀντισθένης ᾤετο εὐήθως μηθὲν ἀξιῶν λέγεσθαι πλὴν τῷ οἰκείῳ λόγῳ, ἓν ἐφ’ ἑνός· ἐξ ὧν συνέ- βαινε μὴ εἶναι ἀντιλέγειν, σχεδὸν δὲ μηδὲ ψεύδεσθαι. ἔστι δ’ ἕκαστον λέγειν οὐ μόνον τῷ αὐτοῦ λόγῳ ἀλλὰ καὶ τῷ (35) ἑτέρου, ψευδῶς μὲν καὶ παντελῶς, ἔστι δ’ ὡς καὶ ἀληθῶς, (1025a) ὥσπερ τὰ ὀκτὼ διπλάσια τῷ τῆς δυάδος λόγῳ. τὰ μὲν οὖν οὕτω λέγεται ψευδῆ, ἄνθρωπος δὲ ψευδὴς ὁ εὐχερὴς καὶ προαιρετικὸς τῶν τοιούτων λόγων, μὴ δι’ ἕτερόν τι ἀλλὰ δι’ αὐτό, καὶ ὁ ἄλλοις ἐμποιητικὸς τῶν τοιούτων λόγων, ὥσπερ καὶ τὰ πράγματά φαμεν ψευδῆ εἶναι ὅσα ἐμποιεῖ (5) φαντασίαν ψευδῆ. διὸ ὁ ἐν τῷ Ἱππίᾳ λόγος παρακρούεται ὡς ὁ αὐτὸς ψευδὴς καὶ ἀληθής. τὸν δυνάμενον γὰρ ψεύ- σασθαι λαμβάνει ψευδῆ (οὗτος δ’ ὁ εἰδὼς καὶ ὁ φρόνι- μος)· ἔτι τὸν ἑκόντα φαῦλον βελτίω. τοῦτο δὲ ψεῦδος λαμβάνει διὰ τῆς ἐπαγωγῆς-ὁ γὰρ ἑκὼν χωλαίνων τοῦ (10) ἄκοντος κρείττων-τὸ χωλαίνειν τὸ μιμεῖσθαι λέγων, ἐπεὶ εἴ γε χωλὸς ἑκών, χείρων ἴσως, ὥσπερ ἐπὶ τοῦ ἤθους, καὶ οὗτος.

<$DEFINITION,metaphysics,Metaphysics@87.0,PRIMARY@423.0,section@17,ontological,Metaphysics/text,definition@1.1,bekker@1024.2,chapter@29,book@5,text@Metaphysics >
<@319.1@ {DEFINITION} {definition@1.1} {ontological} 
False is meant in one way in the sense that a thing is false, and in this way one sort of falsity is because then thing doesn't go together or cannot be put together (in the way that one claims the diagonal to be commensurable or you to be sitting down, for one of these is false and the other sometimes, since these things are not that way), 
Τὸ ψεῦδος λέγεται ἄλλον μὲν τρόπον ὡς πρᾶγμα ψεῦδος, καὶ τούτου τὸ μὲν τῷ μὴ συγκεῖσθαι ἢ ἀδύνατον εἶναι συντεθῆναι (ὥσπερ λέγεται τὸ τὴν διάμετρον εἶναι σύμμετρον ἢ τὸ σὲ καθῆσθαι· τούτων γὰρ ψεῦδος τὸ μὲν (20) ἀεὶ τὸ δὲ ποτέ· οὕτω γὰρ οὐκ ὄντα ταῦτα)

<$PRIMARY@424.0,DEFINITION,metaphysics,section@20,false,ontological,Metaphysics/text,definition@1.2,bekker@1024.2,falsity,Metaphysics@88.0,chapter@29,book@5,text@Metaphysics >
<@319.2@ {DEFINITION} {falsity} {definition@1.2} {ontological} 
and another sort includes things which are, but which naturally seem to be either of a sort that they are not, or things things that are not (for instance a perspective drawing or dreams, for these are something, but not what they produce the appearance of). 

_τὰ δὲ ὅσα ἔστι μὲν ὄντα, πέφυκε μέντοι φαίνεσθαι ἢ μὴ οἷά ἐστιν ἢ ἃ μὴ ἔστιν (οἷον ἡ σκιαγραφία καὶ τὰ ἐνύπνια· ταῦτα γὰρ ἔστι μέν τι, ἀλλ’ οὐχ ὧν ἐμποιεῖ τὴν φαντασίαν)· 
// SUMMARIZING BOTH DEFINITIONS 
Things then are said to be {false} in this way, either because they are not, or because the appearance of them is of something that is not.

<$definition@2.2,logical,Metaphysics@89.0,true,chapter@29,statement,DEFINITION,definition,section@25,PRIMARY@425.0,definition@2.1,logos,book@5,text@Metaphysics,Aristhenes/philosopher,false,bekker@1024.2,falsity,ti hēn einai/syntagm,metaphysics,Metaphysics/text,accidents >
<@319.3@ {falsity} {false} {DEFINITION} {definition@2.1} 
Now a statement is false which is about things that are not, insofar as it is false, for which reason every statement is false about something other than that about which it is {true}, as what is true of the circle is false about the triangle. And of each thiung there is a sense in whcih there is one statement, which say what it is for it to be, but there is another sense in whcih there are many statements, since the thing itself and thing when it has been affected are in a certain way the same, such as Socrates and the educated Socrates (but a false statement is not a statement about anything simply). 
πράγματα μὲν οὖν ψευδῆ οὕτω λέγεται, ἢ τῷ μὴ εἶναι αὐτὰ ἢ τῷ (25) τὴν ἀπ’ αὐτῶν φαντασίαν μὴ ὄντος εἶναι· λόγος δὲ ψευ- @1 δὴς ὁ τῶν μὴ ὄντων, ᾗ ψευδής, διὸ πᾶς λόγος ψευδὴς ἑτέ- ρου ἢ οὗ ἐστὶν ἀληθής, οἷον ὁ τοῦ κύκλου ψευδὴς τριγώνου.
ἑκάστου δὲ λόγος ἔστι μὲν ὡς εἷς, ὁ τοῦ τί ἦν εἶναι, ἔστι δ’ ὡς πολλοί, ἐπεὶ ταὐτό πως αὐτὸ καὶ αὐτὸ πεπονθός, οἷον Σω- (30) κράτης καὶ Σωκράτης μουσικός (ὁ δὲ ψευδὴς λόγος οὐθενός ἐστιν ἁπλῶς λόγος)· [I've included the Greek for the conclusion of the first definitions, since the transition is important] 

For this reason Aristhenes believed simplemindedly that nothing was fit to be said except a thing's own articulation, one statement for one thing, from which it followed that there could be no contradicting, and practically no making a mistake.

But it is possible to speak of each thing not only by its articulation but also by that of something else, and this either completely falsely, or it is possible in a way also truly, as eight is called doubt by means of the articulation of two.

{definition@2.1} = {logical} truth of the {definition} itself. The {statement} {logos} of the {ti hēn einai/syntagm} 
{definition@2.2} [THESE aren't really so clearly separated though] = {logical} truth of statements said about the {accidents} of something 
// If, as for {Aristhenes/philosopher} only the statement of the articulation is the only thing fit to be said, then nothing would be falsely said. [Every definition is either completely true, or misses its mark so much that it is not even false...]

<$DEFINITION,metaphysics,Hippias/Socratic dialogue,false,Metaphysics@90.0,extended,metaphorical,Metaphysics/text,disability,limping,definition@3.1,bekker@1025.1,true,PRIMARY@426.0,chapter@29,book@5,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@319.4@ {DEFINITION} {false} {definition@3.1} {metaphorical} {extended}? 
and a false human being is one who skillfully and deliberately makes use of such statements., for no other reason but for its own sake, and who foists such statements onto other people, in the same way that we also said that things are false which produce a false appearance. 
For this reason the arguement in the {Hippias/Socratic dialogue=dialogue=text} goes wrong in saying tha tthe same person is {false} and {true}. For it takes the one who is capable of deceiving as being false (and this is the one who knows and has understanding) and further assumes that the one who willingly does low things is better than one who does so unwillingly. But this takes something false out of a surver of examples, for the one who limps willingly is better off than the one who does so unwillingly if limping means imitating a limp, since if one is in fact willingly lame he is perhaps worse off, just as in the case of moral character, so this one too.

{disability} {limping} 
τὰ μὲν οὖν οὕτω λέγεται ψευδῆ, ἄνθρωπος δὲ ψευδὴς ὁ εὐχερὴς καὶ προαιρετικὸς τῶν τοιούτων λόγων, μὴ δι’ ἕτερόν τι ἀλλὰ δι’ αὐτό, καὶ ὁ ἄλλοις ἐμποιητικὸς τῶν τοιούτων λόγων, ὥσπερ καὶ τὰ πράγματά φαμεν ψευδῆ εἶναι ὅσα ἐμποιεῖ (5) φαντασίαν ψευδῆ. διὸ ὁ ἐν τῷ Ἱππίᾳ λόγος παρακρούεται ὡς ὁ αὐτὸς ψευδὴς καὶ ἀληθής. τὸν δυνάμενον γὰρ ψεύ- σασθαι λαμβάνει ψευδῆ (οὗτος δ’ ὁ εἰδὼς καὶ ὁ φρόνι- μος)· ἔτι τὸν ἑκόντα φαῦλον βελτίω. τοῦτο δὲ ψεῦδος λαμβάνει διὰ τῆς ἐπαγωγῆς-ὁ γὰρ ἑκὼν χωλαίνων τοῦ (10) ἄκοντος κρείττων-τὸ χωλαίνειν τὸ μιμεῖσθαι λέγων, ἐπεὶ εἴ γε χωλὸς ἑκών, χείρων ἴσως, ὥσπερ ἐπὶ τοῦ ἤθους, καὶ οὗτος.

<$314,314.28,metaphysics,Metaphysics@91.0,chapter@5,excluded middle,book@9,Metaphysics/text,314.28.1,compare@Metaphysics Gamma,bekker@1062.1,PRIMARY@427.0,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@320@ FROM Metaphysics Kappa, Book 9, Chapter 5 

[Sachs] There is a certain principle in beings about which it is not possible to be mistaken, but always necessary to do the opposite, and I mean to attain the truth, and it is of this sort: that it is impossible for the same thing at one and the same time to be and not to be, and for the other things that are opposite to each other in that way to belong to it.

Ἔστι δέ τις ἐν τοῖς οὖσιν ἀρχὴ περὶ ἣν οὐκ ἔστι διεψεῦ- σθαι, τοὐναντίον δὲ ἀναγκαῖον ἀεὶ ποιεῖν, λέγω δὲ ἀληθεύειν, (35) οἷον ὅτι οὐκ ἐνδέχεται τὸ αὐτὸ καθ’ ἕνα καὶ τὸν αὐτὸν χρό- (1062a) νον εἶναι καὶ μὴ εἶναι, καὶ τἆλλα τὰ τοῦτον αὑτοῖς ἀντι- κείμενα τὸν τρόπον. καὶ περὶ τῶν τοιούτων ἁπλῶς μὲν οὐκ ἔστιν ἀπόδειξις, πρὸς τόνδε δὲ ἔστιν· 

law of the {excluded middle} 
THE ARGUMENT GIVEN HERE SEEMS SLIGHTLY DIFFERENT THAN {compare@Metaphysics Gamma}...

It is necessary then for each of the words to be intelligible and to mean something, not many things but only one, but if it does mean more than one thing, it is necessary to make clear to which of these one is applying the word. So the one one who say "this is and is not" denies that which he says, and so he denies that this word means what it means, which is impossible. So if being-this means something, it is impossible for the contradiction to be true.

_ὁ δὴ λέγων εἶναι τοῦτο καὶ μὴ εἶναι, τοῦτο ὅ φησιν οὔ φησιν, ὥσθ’ ὃ σημαίνει τοὔνομα τοῦτ’ οὔ φησι σημαίνειν· τοῦτο δ’ ἀδύνατον. ὥστ’ εἴπερ σημαίνει τι τὸ εἶναι τόδε, τὴν ἀντίφασιν ἀδύνατον ἀληθεύειν. ἔτι δ’ εἴ τι σημαίνει τοὔνομα καὶ τοῦτ’ ἀληθεύεται, δεῖ τοῦτ’ ἐξ ἀνάγκης (20) εἶναι· τὸ δ’ ἐξ ἀνάγκης ὂν οὐκ ἐνδέχεταί ποτε μὴ εἶναι· 

Also if nothing is truly asserted, even this itself would be false...

<$chapter@10,metaphysics,Metaphysics@92.0,law of the excluded middle,Truth,book@9,Metaphysics/text,bekker@1051.2,SUMMARY,PRIMARY@428.0,truth,section@1,text@Metaphysics >
<@321@ THE DISCUSSION OF {Truth} in Metaphysics Theta,  book 9, chapter 10... 

And since being and not being are meant in  one sense  by reference to the various ways  of attributing being,  and in another by reference  to the potency or being-at-work  of these or  their opposites, but the most governing  sense  is the true or the false, and since this as   applied to particular things depends on combining   or separating, in such a way that one who  thinks  what is separated is separated and  what is combined  is combined thinks truly,  but one who thinks these  things to be opposite  to the way the things are things  falsely,  when is what is spoken of as truth or falsity   present or not present? For it is necessary  to examine  in what way we mnean this. For  you are not pale because  we think truly that  you are pale, but rather it is  because you  are pale that we who say so speak the  truth.  So if some things are always combined and   incapable of being separated, while other are  always  separate and incapable of being combined,  then others  admit of opposites (for being  something is being-combined  and being-one,  while not being it is not being combined  but  being more than one).   
Concerning the ones that admit of opposites,  then,  the same opinion and the same statement  come to be  false and true, and it is possible  for someone at  one time to think truly and  at another time to think  falsely; but about  the ones that are incapable of  being otherwise,  nothing comes to be at one time  true, at another  time false, but the same things  are always  true and false.  

 Ἐπεὶ δὲ τὸ ὂν λέγεται καὶ τὸ μὴ ὂν τὸ μὲν κατὰ τὰ 



σχήματα τῶν κατηγοριῶν, τὸ δὲ κατὰ δύναμιν  ἢ ἐνέρ-  (35) (1051b) γειαν τούτων ἢ τἀναντία,  τὸ δὲ [κυριώτατα  ὂν] ἀληθὲς ἢ ψεῦδος, τοῦτο  δ’ ἐπὶ τῶν πραγμάτων ἐστὶ  τῷ συγκεῖσθαι ἢ  διῃρῆσθαι, ὥστε ἀληθεύει μὲν ὁ τὸ  διῃρημένον  οἰόμενος διῃ- ρῆσθαι καὶ τὸ συγκείμενον  συγκεῖσθαι,  ἔψευσται δὲ ὁ ἐναν- τίως ἔχων ἢ τὰ πράγματα,   πότ’ ἔστιν ἢ οὐκ ἔστι τὸ ἀληθὲς (5) λεγόμενον  ἢ ψεῦδος;  τοῦτο γὰρ σκεπτέον τί λέγομεν. οὐ  γὰρ διὰ τὸ ἡμᾶς  οἴεσθαι ἀληθῶς σε λευκὸν εἶναι  εἶ σὺ λευκός, ἀλλὰ  διὰ τὸ σὲ εἶναι λευκὸν  ἡμεῖς οἱ φάντες τοῦτο ἀληθεύομεν.  εἰ δὴ τὰ  μὲν ἀεὶ σύγκειται καὶ ἀδύνατα δι- αιρεθῆναι,   τὰ δ’ ἀεὶ διῄρηται καὶ ἀδύνατα συντεθῆναι,  τὰ (10)  δ’ ἐνδέχεται τἀναντία, τὸ μὲν εἶναί  ἐστι τὸ συγκεῖσθαι  καὶ ἓν εἶναι, τὸ δὲ μὴ  εἶναι τὸ μὴ συγκεῖσθαι ἀλλὰ  πλείω εἶναι· περὶ  μὲν οὖν τὰ ἐνδεχόμενα ἡ αὐτὴ γίγνεται  ψευδὴς  καὶ ἀληθὴς δόξα καὶ ὁ λόγος ὁ αὐτός, καὶ ἐνδέχεται   ὁτὲ μὲν ἀληθεύειν ὁτὲ δὲ ψεύδεσθαι· περὶ δὲ  τὰ ἀδύνατα  ἄλ- (15) λως ἔχειν οὐ γίγνεται  ὁτὲ μὲν ἀληθὲς ὁτὲ  δὲ ψεῦδος, ἀλλ’ @1 ἀεὶ  ταὐτὰ ἀληθῆ καὶ ψευδῆ.
